Repetition of Oaths: Yamin Billah vs. Talaq
Distinguishes yamin billah from talaq/itaq on repetition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between oaths by Allah and conditional talaq/itaq regarding repetition and explain the underlying principle that a consequence suspended on a repeated and non-repeated condition does not repeat.

Core Idea
A condition that is both repeated and non-repeated triggers multiple talaqs but only one yamin billah.

In Hanafi fiqh, the type of oath determines how repetition works when a condition is mixed. This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies that oaths by Allah require both parts of a condition to exist together, so they do not repeat, while talaq and itaq are triggered by the repeated condition alone. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying rulings on broken oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of oaths
Recognize that the text deals with oaths by Allah (yamin billah) and personal oaths (talaq, itaq, zihar). Each has different rules for repetition.
2
Classify the condition components
Note that the condition in the examples has two parts: a repeated condition (e.g., 'every time you enter') and a non-repeated condition (e.g., 'if I speak to so-and-so').
3
Analyze the validity requirement for yamin billah
In yamin billah, the oath requires both the repeated and non-repeated conditions to exist together for validity. Since the non-repeated condition occurs only once, the oath does not repeat.
4
Analyze the validity requirement for talaq/itaq
In talaq and itaq, the oath is valid with only the repeated condition. Therefore, each occurrence of the repeated condition triggers a separate oath.
5
Apply the general principle
The text concludes that a consequence suspended on a repeated and non-repeated condition does not repeat with the repetition of the repeated condition alone, because both conditions must exist for the consequence to occur.
This principle applies to hajj obligations as well.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر في الولوالجية : الطلاق والعتاق متى علق بشرط متكرر يتكرر، واليمين متى علق بشرط متكرر لا يتكرر حتى لو قال كلما دخلت الدار فوالله لا أكلم فلاناً فدخلت الدار مراراً فكلمه بعد ذلك لا يحنث إلا في يمين واحدة. ولو قال كلما دخلت الدار فأنت طالق إن كلمت فلاناً فدخل الدار مراراً ثم كلمه مرة يحنث في الأيمان كلها والفرق أن انعقاد اليمين بالله ليس إلا ذكر اسم الله تعالى مقروناً بخبر وذكر اسم الله تعالى مقرون بخبر الدخول والكلام ، فكما أن لانعقاد اليمين تعلقا بالدخول كان لها تعلق بالكلام بدليل أنه لو قال إن دخلت والله ولم يقل لا أكلم لا ينعقد فلم ينفسخ ليكن تصحيح اليمين بالله تعالى معلقاً بالدخول وحده وإنما تصحيحها بالدخول والكلام جميعاً والدخول متكرر والكلام غير متكرر والمعلق بشرط متكرر وغير متكرر لا يتكرر فأما اليمين بالطلاق والعتاق وغيرهما فمعلق بالدخول وحده ، ألا ترى أنه لو اقتصر عليه صح فلم يكن لانعقاد اليمين تعلق بالكلام فيبقى اليمين معلقاً بالدخول وحده والدخول يتكرر لأنه أدخل فيه كلمة «كلما» والمعلق بشرط متكرر يتكرر فيصير قائلاً عند كل دخلة إن كلمت فلاناً فامرأته طالق، ولو كرر هذه المقالة ثم كلمه مرة يحنث في الأيمان كلها لأن الشرط الواحد يصلح شرطاً للأيمان كلها ا هـ . وزاد البزازي على الطلاق والعتاق الظهار. وفي المحيط معزياً إلى الجامع : أصله أن الجزاء متى علق بشرط مكرر وغير مكرر فإنه لا يتكرر بتكرر المكرر لأن المعلق بشرطين لا ينزل إلا عند وجودهما، فلو قال كلما دخلت هذه الدار فعلي حجة إن ضربتك فدخل مراراً ولم يضربه إلا مرة فإنه يلزمه الحج بعد الدخلات لأن المعلق بالشرط كالمرسل عند وجود الشرط فكأنه قال عند كل دخلة علي حجة إن ضربتك بخلاف ما لو ضربه ودخل ثم دخل مرة أخرى فإنه لا يلزمه حجة أخرى ما لم يضربه ثانياً، وكذلك لو قال كلما دخلت الدار فامرأته طالق وعبده حر إن ضربت فلاناً لأنه علق بشرط مكرر وهو الدخول عتقاً أو طلاقاً معلقاً بالضرب ا.هـ.
And it is mentioned in al-Walwaljiyya: Talaq and itaq whenever they are suspended by a repeated condition, they repeat; and the oath (yamin) whenever suspended by a repeated condition, it does not repeat. Even if he said: Whenever you enter the house, by Allah I will not speak to so-and-so, and she entered the house multiple times and he spoke to him after that, he is not sinful except for one oath. And if he said: Whenever you enter the house, you are divorced if you speak to so-and-so, and she entered the house multiple times and then spoke to him once, he is sinful for all the oaths. The difference is that the contract of the oath by Allah is only the mention of Allah's name connected to a report, and the mention of Allah's name connected to the report of entering and speaking. So just as the contract of the oath has a connection to entering, it has a connection to speaking, as evidence that if he said 'If you enter, by Allah' and did not say 'I will not speak', it does not contract; so it was not valid [؟]. Rather, the validation of the oath by Allah is suspended on entering alone, but its validation is by entering and speaking together, and entering is repeated while speaking is not repeated, and what is suspended by a repeated and non-repeated condition does not repeat. As for the oath by talaq and itaq and others, it is suspended on entering alone. Do you not see that if he limited it to that, it would be valid? So there is no connection of the oath to speaking. Thus the oath remains suspended on entering alone, and entering is repeated because he included the word 'kullama', and what is suspended by a repeated condition repeats. So it becomes as if he said at each entry: If I speak to so-and-so, his wife is divorced. And if he repeated this statement and then spoke to him once, he is sinful for all the oaths because one condition suffices as a condition for all the oaths. End. And al-Bazzazi added to talaq and itaq the zihar. And in al-Muhit, attributing to al-Jami': The principle is that the consequence (jaza') when suspended by a repeated and non-repeated condition does not repeat by the repetition of the repeated, because what is suspended by two conditions does not occur except when both exist. So if he said: Whenever you enter this house, I owe a hajj if I hit you, and he entered multiple times but did not hit you except once, he is obligated for the hajj after the entries because what is suspended by the condition is like a sent (conditional) statement when the condition occurs; it is as if he said at each entry: I owe a hajj if I hit you. Unlike if he hit you and entered, then entered again, he is not obligated for another hajj unless he hits you a second time. And similarly if he said: Whenever you enter the house, his wife is divorced and his slave is free if I hit so-and-so, because he suspended by a repeated condition (entering) the freeings or divorces suspended by hitting. End.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal analysis of how repetition works in oaths. It distinguishes between oaths by Allah (yamin billah) and conditional divorces/emancipations. The key insight: when a condition is both repeated (like 'every time you enter') and non-repeated (like 'if I hit you'), the oath by Allah does not repeat, because the oath's validity requires both the repeated and non-repeated conditions to exist together. Since the non-repeated condition only occurs once, the oath is not triggered multiple times. In contrast, with talaq and itaq, the repeated condition alone suffices for the oath's validity, so each occurrence of the repeated condition triggers a new oath. The passage also includes an example from al-Bazzazi adding zihar to the ruling, and from al-Muhit explaining the principle: if the consequence is suspended on two conditions, one repeated and one non-repeated, it does not repeat because both must exist. However, if the consequence is suspended on the repeated condition alone, or if the non-repeated condition is embedded in a way that it is considered separate for each occurrence, repetition occurs. The commentary clarifies the Hanafi position that the distinction is based on the structure of the oath: in yamin billah, the mention of Allah requires the conjunction of entering and speaking, making the non-repeated condition essential for each occurrence; in talaq, the oath contracts on entering alone, so it repeats.

Key Terms
يمين بالله (yamin billah): An oath by Allah, which requires both the repeated and non-repeated conditions for its validity, so it does not repeat.
شرط متكرر (shart mutakarrir): A condition that can occur multiple times, such as 'whenever you enter'.
شرط غير متكرر (shart ghayr mutakarrir): A condition that occurs only once, such as 'if I hit you once'.
جزاء (jaza'): The consequence that is made conditional, such as divorce, emancipation, or obligation of hajj.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the resulting ruling if someone says, 'Whenever you enter the house, you are divorced if I hit Zaid' and the wife enters three times and the husband hits Zaid once?
A) One divorce occurs
B) Three divorces occur
C) No divorce occurs until the husband hits Zaid again
D) It depends on intention

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, oaths by Allah do not repeat when the condition is mixed, but conditional talaq and itaq do repeat with the repeated condition.

Kullama with Indefinite Women: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains the ruling of 'kullama' with indefinite women and critiques Abu Yusuf's view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the dominant Hanafi position that 'kullama' triggers a new divorce for each marriage even for indefinite women, and explain why Abu Yusuf's opposing view is rejected.

Core Idea
'Kullama' governs actions, not names—each marriage, even of the same woman, triggers a new divorce.

The Arabic particle 'kullama' often implies repetition, but its scope in conditional divorce statements is disputed within the Hanafi school. Abu Yusuf held that when the woman is indefinite (e.g., 'a woman'), the oath dissolves after one divorce per woman, like 'kull'. The dominant view, however, insists on repetition: every marriage—whether of a new woman or the same woman after remarriage—triggers a fresh divorce. This lesson unpacks the linguistic and legal reasoning behind the conflict.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and the issue
Read the conditional statement: 'Whenever I marry a woman, she is divorced.' Focus on the dispute: does this cause one divorce per woman ever, or one divorce per marriage act?
2
State the dominant Hanafi position
The text's author (and the school's relied-upon view) holds that each marriage (even remarrying the same woman) triggers a new divorce because 'kullama' gives generality of actions, not names.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Present Abu Yusuf's opposing view
Abu Yusuf argues that 'kullama' with indefinite women behaves like 'kull' (every), so once a specific woman is divorced, the oath is spent for that woman. Only definite women ('Zaynab') cause repetition on remarriage.
His view is from al-Muntaqa and is a minority within the school.
4
Analyse the rebuttal
The author rejects Abu Yusuf's analogy by noting that 'kullama' applies to actions, and actions (marriages) can be repeated by a single person indefinitely. The circle of actions is wider than that of names, so each action triggers its own consequence.
The key distinction: between 'kullama' (action-generality) and 'kull' (name-generality).
5
Contrast with 'kull'
If the husband said 'Every woman I marry is divorced' (using 'kull'), then each new woman gets one divorce, but remarrying the same woman does not cause another divorce because 'kull' covers names, not repeated actions.
This contrast clarifies why 'kullama' is different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فلو قال كلما تزوجت امرأة يحنث بكل امرأة ولو بعد زوج آخر) بيان لبعض تفاريع «كل» و«كلما وهي مسائل منها مسألة الكتاب ووجهه أن الشرط ملك يوجد في المستقبل وهو غير محصور وكلما أوجد هذا الشرط تبعه ملك الثلاث فيتبعه جزاؤه. وحاصل ما ذهب إليه أبو يوسف أن «كلما إنما توجب التكرار في المعينة لا في غير المعينة بادعاء اتحاد الحاصل بين «كل» و «كلما إذا نسب فعلها إلى منكر متكرر لأن الحاصل كل تزوج لكل امرأة وفي مثله تنقسم الآحاد فلزم بالضرورة أنها إذا انحلت في فعل انحلت في اسمه فلا يتكرر الحنث في امرأة واحدة وهو مردود لانقسام الآحاد على الآحاد عند التساوي وهو منتف لأن دائرة عموم الأفعال أوسع لأن كثيراً من أفراده ما يتحقق بالتكرار من شخص واحد وقد فرض عمومه بـ«كلما» فلا يعتبر كل اسم بفعل واحد فقط . ومنها لو قال كل امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق فكل امرأة تزوجها تطلق واحدة فإن تزوجها ثانياً لا تطلق لاقتضائها عموما الأسماء لا عموم الأفعال . قوله : (وحاصل ما ذهب إليه أبو يوسف الخ) كان الأنسب . ، ذكر قوله قبل التخريج. وذكره في الفتح فقال : وعن أبي يوسف في المنتقى : إذا قال كلما تزوجت امرأة فهي طالق فتزوج امرأة طلقت،
His statement: (If he said 'Whenever I marry a woman, he is sinful for every woman, even after another husband') is an explanation of some branches of 'kull' and 'kullama'. Among them is the issue of the book. Its face is that the condition is ownership that occurs in the future, and it is unlimited. And whenever he produces this condition, the ownership of three [divorces] follows it, so its consequence follows. The summary of what Abu Yusuf went to is that 'kullama' only necessitates repetition in the definite (al-mu'ayyana), not in the indefinite (ghayr al-mu'ayyana), claiming the unity of the result between 'kull' and 'kullama' when its action is attributed to an indefinite repeated [action] because the result is every marriage for every woman. In such a case, the singulars are divided, so necessarily if it dissolves in an action, it dissolves in its name, so there is no repetition of sin in one woman. And it is rebutted because the division of singulars over singulars [works only] when they are equal, which is absent because the circle of generality of actions is wider, since many of its individuals occur by repetition from a single person, and it is presumed general by 'kullama', so not every name is considered by only one action. Among them: if he said 'Every woman I marry is divorced', then every woman he marries is divorced once; if he marries her a second time, she is not divorced because it necessitates generality of names, not generality of actions. His statement: (The summary of what Abu Yusuf went to...) was more appropriate to mention his statement before the derivation. And he mentioned it in al-Fath and said: From Abu Yusuf in al-Muntaqa: If he said 'Whenever I marry a woman, she is divorced', and he married a woman, she is divorced. [End]
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the scope of 'kullama' when applied to indefinite women (ghayr mu'ayyana). The author explains that if someone says 'Whenever I marry a woman, she is divorced', then according to the dominant view (which is the basis of the text), he is sinful for every woman he marries, even if he remarries a woman after a previous divorce. Each new marriage triggers a new divorce. Abu Yusuf, however, holds that 'kullama' only causes repetition when the woman is definite (already specified), not when the woman is indefinite. His reasoning is that 'kullama' here is like 'kull' (every), so each marriage is tied to a specific woman, and once the divorce occurs, the oath is exhausted for that woman. The author rebuts this by arguing that the generality of actions (umum al-af'al) is broader: one person can perform many actions (marriages) over time, and 'kullama' extends to each action, not just each name. The text then contrasts with 'kull': if one says 'Every woman I marry is divorced', then each new woman gets one divorce, but remarrying the same woman does not cause another divorce because 'kull' covers names, not repeated actions. The passage ends with a note that it would be more appropriate to state Abu Yusuf's exact wording before the rebuttal, and quotes al-Fath citing Abu Yusuf's view: 'Whenever I marry a woman, she is divorced' — he marries a woman, she is divorced (implying no repetition for subsequent marriages of the same woman?). The quote is cut off, but the dispute is clear.

Key Terms
كلما (kullama): Particle for repeated actions; in this context, with 'tazawwaju imra'a', it triggers a new divorce for each marriage.
معينة (mu'ayyana): A definite, specific woman (e.g., 'Fatima') about whom the oath was made.
غير معينة (ghayr mu'ayyana): An indefinite woman, not specified; the oath covers any woman he marries.
أبو يوسف (Abu Yusuf): The prominent Hanafi jurist and student of Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No second divorce occurs
B) A second divorce occurs
C) Only one total divorce
D) It depends on intention

Key Takeaway
The distinction between 'kullama' (action-generality) and 'kull' (name-generality) is key to understanding this intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional divorce.

Effect of 'Kullama' in Suspended Oaths
Expansion on 'kullama' in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why 'kullama' in a divorce oath causes the divorce to occur with each occurrence of the condition, and distinguish this from oaths by Allah.

Core Idea
'Kullama' makes a condition repetitive; the consequence repeats with each instance.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on the difference between oaths by Allah and oaths by divorce/emancipation when the particle 'kullama' (whenever) is used. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct application of suspended talaq and emancipation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of oaths
Compare oaths by Allah with oaths by divorce/emancipation as presented in the text.
Oath by Allah requires both entry and speech; divorce/emancipation requires only entry.
2
Understand validity conditions
Note that for an oath by Allah, if only entry is mentioned, the oath is invalid. For divorce/emancipation, it is valid with only entry.
3
Analyze the role of 'kullama'
'Kullama' makes the condition (entry) repetitive, so the consequence repeats with each occurrence.
Without 'kullama', the consequence occurs only once upon the first fulfillment.
4
Derive the ruling for multiple conditions
When a consequence is suspended on both a repetitive and non-repetitive condition, it does not repeat with the repetitive alone; both must be met for each repetition.
Example: 'Whenever I enter, I owe a hajj if I hit you' – hitting is non-repetitive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب التعليق
٢٧
كلما تزوجت امرأة يحنث بكل امرأة ولو بعد زوج آخر وزوال الملك بعد اليمين لا يبطلها فإن وجد الشرط في الملك
بخبر وذكر اسم الله تعالى مقرون بخبر الدخول والكلام ، فكما أن لانعقاد اليمين تعلقا بالدخول كان لها تعلق بالكلام بدليل أنه لو قال إن دخلت والله ولم يقل لا أكلم لا ينعقد فلم ينفسخ ليكن تصحيح اليمين بالله تعالى معلقاً بالدخول وحده وإنما تصحيحها بالدخول والكلام جميعاً والدخول متكرر والكلام غير متكرر والمعلق بشرط متكرر وغير متكرر لا يتكرر فأما اليمين بالطلاق والعتاق وغيرهما فمعلق بالدخول وحده ، ألا ترى أنه لو اقتصر عليه صح فلم يكن لانعقاد اليمين تعلق بالكلام فيبقى اليمين معلقاً بالدخول وحده والدخول يتكرر لأنه أدخل فيه كلمة «كلما» والمعلق بشرط متكرر يتكرر فيصير قائلاً عند كل دخلة إن كلمت فلاناً فامرأته طالق، ولو كرر هذه المقالة ثم كلمه مرة يحنث في الأيمان كلها لأن الشرط الواحد يصلح شرطاً للأيمان كلها ا هـ . وزاد البزازي على الطلاق والعتاق الظهار. وفي المحيط معزياً إلى الجامع : أصله أن الجزاء متى علق بشرط مكرر وغير مكرر فإنه لا يتكرر بتكرر المكرر لأن المعلق بشرطين لا ينزل إلا عند وجودهما، فلو قال كلما دخلت هذه الدار فعلي حجة إن ضربتك فدخل مراراً ولم يضربه إلا مرة فإنه يلزمه الحج بعد الدخلات لأن المعلق بالشرط كالمرسل عند وجود الشرط فكأنه قال عند كل دخلة علي حجة إن ضربتك بخلاف ما لو ضربه ودخل ثم دخل مرة أخرى فإنه لا يلزمه حجة أخرى ما لم يضربه ثانياً، وكذلك لو قال كلما دخلت الدار فامرأته طالق وعبده حر إن ضربت فلاناً لأنه علق بشرط مكرر وهو الدخول عتقاً أو طلاقاً معلقاً بالضرب ا.هـ.
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Suspension. Whenever I marry a woman, he is sworn regarding every woman, even after another husband and after ownership ends; the oath is not invalidated by the end of ownership. If the condition is found in ownership, by the utterance and mention of the name of Allah the Exalted connected to the utterance of entry and speech. Just as the oath's validity is connected to entry, it is also connected to speech, evidenced by the fact that if he said 'By Allah I enter' and did not say 'I will not speak', it does not take effect. So the oath by Allah the Exalted is not established suspended only on entry; rather its validity is by both entry and speech. Entry is repetitive, speech is not repetitive, and what is suspended on a repetitive and non-repetitive condition does not repeat. As for the oath by divorce, emancipation, etc., it is suspended on entry alone. Do you not see that if he confined it to entry, it is valid; thus the oath's validity has no connection to speech, so the oath remains suspended on entry alone. Entry is repetitive because he included the word 'kullama', and what is suspended on a repetitive condition repeats. So he becomes saying at each entry: 'If I speak to so-and-so, his wife is divorced.' And if he repeated this statement then spoke to him once, he violates all the oaths because the single condition suffices as a condition for all the oaths. Al-Bazzazi added to divorce and emancipation the case of zihar. And in al-Muhit, attributed to al-Jami': The principle is that when the consequence is suspended on a repeated and a non-repeated condition, it does not repeat with the repetition of the repeated, because what is suspended on two conditions only occurs when both exist. If he said 'Whenever I enter this house, I owe a hajj if I hit you', and he entered repeatedly but only hit once, hajj becomes obligatory after the entries because what is suspended on the condition is like unbound upon the existence of the condition; as if he said at each entry 'I owe a hajj if I hit you'. Contrast: if he hit and entered, then entered again, no further hajj is due unless he hits a second time. Similarly, if he said 'Whenever I enter the house, his wife is divorced and his slave is free if I hit so-and-so', because he suspended emancipation or divorce on a repeated condition (entry) while also suspended on hitting.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn explaining the effect of using 'kullama' (whenever) in suspended oaths. The key distinction is between oaths by Allah and oaths by divorce/emancipation. For oaths by Allah, the oath is only valid if both the condition of entry and the condition of speech are expressed; if only entry is mentioned, the oath is invalid. For divorce/emancipation oaths, the oath is valid even if only entry is mentioned as the condition. When 'kullama' is used, the condition (entry) is considered repetitive, so the consequence (divorce) occurs with each occurrence of entry. Even if the speaker repeats the entire oath many times and then fulfills the condition once, he violates all the oaths because the single occurrence of the condition triggers all the oaths simultaneously. Al-Bazzazi adds that the same applies to zihar. The passage from al-Muhit clarifies a subtle point: when a consequence is suspended on both a repeated and a non-repeated condition (e.g., entry and hitting), it does not repeat with repetition of the repeated condition alone; both conditions must be met for each occurrence. For example, if one says 'Whenever I enter this house, I owe a hajj if I hit you', and enters multiple times but hits only once, hajj is due only once because the hitting condition is not repeated. However, if the hitting is also suspended on the repeated condition (as in the final example), the consequence repeats with each entry because the hitting is not an independent condition but part of the suspension.

Key Terms
كلما (kullama): A particle meaning 'whenever', used to suspend a consequence on a recurring condition, causing repetition of the consequence with each occurrence.
يمين (yamin): An oath; a solemn declaration that suspends a consequence (like divorce, emancipation, or a vow) on the occurrence of a condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It depends on his intention.
D) No divorce occurs until he speaks as well.

Key Takeaway
When 'kullama' is used in a suspended oath of divorce or emancipation, the consequence repeats with each occurrence of the condition, even if the condition occurs after the oath is initially fulfilled.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

The Scope of 'Kullama' in Divorce Oaths
Presents and refutes Abu Yusuf's opinion that 'kullama' only repeats for specific women, not indefinite ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether 'kullama' repeats the divorce for non-specific women and explain why Abu Yusuf's contrary view is rejected.

Core Idea
Kullama triggers repetition for each marriage, even to the same indefinite woman — because it governs actions, not names.

In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the scope of 'kullama' (whenever) in divorce oaths. Imam Abu Yusuf held that 'kullama' only causes repetition when the woman is specified, arguing that for indefinite women it behaves like 'kull' (every). The text clearly rejects this view, affirming that each marriage under a 'kullama' oath triggers a fresh consequence. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying conditional divorce formulations.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's general position on 'kullama'
Recall that 'kullama' makes the condition repetitive; each occurrence of the condition triggers the consequence. For divorce, each marriage to any woman (specific or indefinite) is a separate condition.
This is the established principle from earlier passages.
2
Identify Abu Yusuf's specific objection
Abu Yusuf argued that when the object (woman) is indefinite (nakira), 'kullama' effectively equates to 'kull' (every), so it does not repeat actions for the same woman.
He claimed a unity between 'kull' and 'kullama' when the action is attributed to a repeated indefinite.
3
Analyze the proof: Abu Yusuf's reasoning
Abu Yusuf reasoned that if you say 'Whenever I marry a woman', the result is 'every marriage for every woman'. Since the woman is indefinite, the units (individual women) divide the actions, so marrying the same woman twice only counts as one action.
He based this on the idea that the action dissolves into the name of the object.
4
Examine the Hanafi response
The commentator refutes this by noting that the division of units (actions and names) requires equality, which is absent. The generality of actions is broader: many actions (marriages) can be performed by one person, and 'kullama' imposes generality on actions, not on names.
Thus each marriage is a separate condition regardless of the woman's specificity.
5
Determine the practical consequence and mufta bihi
Under Abu Yusuf's view, marrying the same indefinite woman twice yields only one divorce; under Abu Hanifa's view, it yields two divorces (up to three). The mufta bihi (operative position) is that of Imam Abu Hanifa.
The text explicitly calls Abu Yusuf's view 'refuted' (mardud).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فلو قال كلما تزوجت امرأة يحنث بكل امرأة ولو بعد زوج آخر) بيان لبعض تفاريع «كل» و«كلما وهي مسائل منها مسألة الكتاب ووجهه أن الشرط ملك يوجد في المستقبل وهو غير محصور وكلما أوجد هذا الشرط تبعه ملك الثلاث فيتبعه جزاؤه. وحاصل ما ذهب إليه أبو يوسف أن «كلما إنما توجب التكرار في المعينة لا في غير المعينة بادعاء اتحاد الحاصل بين «كل» و «كلما إذا نسب فعلها إلى منكر متكرر لأن الحاصل كل تزوج لكل امرأة وفي مثله تنقسم الآحاد فلزم بالضرورة أنها إذا انحلت في فعل انحلت في اسمه فلا يتكرر الحنث في امرأة واحدة وهو مردود لانقسام الآحاد على الآحاد عند التساوي وهو منتف لأن دائرة عموم الأفعال أوسع لأن كثيراً من أفراده ما يتحقق بالتكرار من شخص واحد وقد فرض عمومه بـ«كلما» فلا يعتبر كل اسم بفعل واحد فقط .
His statement: (If he says 'Whenever I marry a woman, he is sworn regarding every woman, even after another husband') is an exposition of some branches of 'kull' and 'kullama'. These include the case of the book and its explanation: the condition is ownership which occurs in the future and is not limited; whenever he causes this condition to exist, the ownership of three follows, so its consequence follows. The gist of what Abu Yusuf held is that 'kullama' only necessitates repetition in specific (women), not in non-specific, claiming that the result between 'kull' and 'kullama' is unified when its action is attributed to a repeated indefinite, because the result is 'every marriage for every woman', and in such a case the units divide; it necessarily follows that if they dissolve in an action, they dissolve in its name, so the violation does not repeat in one woman. This is refuted because of the division of units onto units when equal, which is nonexistent; because the scope of the generality of actions is wider, since many of its individuals are realized by repetition from one person, and it has been assumed as general by 'kullama', so every name is not considered by only one action.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the scope of 'kullama' when used with non-specific (indefinite) nouns like 'woman'. The matn states that if one says 'Whenever I marry a woman, she is divorced', the oath applies to every woman married, even after another husband and after ownership ends. The commentator explains that the condition (marriage) is a recurring act, and each occurrence triggers the consequence. Abu Yusuf, however, held that 'kullama' only causes repetition when the woman is specified (e.g., 'Whenever I marry Zaynab'), not when it is indefinite ('a woman'). He argued that 'kull' (every) and 'kullama' (whenever) essentially produce the same effect when the action is attributed to a repeated indefinite: the result is 'every marriage for every woman', so the units (individual women) divide the actions. Thus, for a single woman married multiple times, the violation (divorce) occurs only once for each woman. The commentator rejects this argument, stating that the division of units onto units only applies when they are equal, which is not the case here because the generality of actions is broader: many actions (marriages) can be performed by a single person, and 'kullama' imposes generality on actions, so each action is a separate condition. Therefore, each marriage to the same woman triggers a new divorce.

Key Terms
كلما (kullama): In this context, 'kullama' is argued by Abu Yusuf to only necessitate repetition for specified (ma'ayyana) objects, not indefinite ones.
مندرج (mudraj): Not used directly; but the concept of 'indefinite repeated' (nakira mutakarrir) is central.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, a man says to an unspecified woman, 'Whenever I marry you, you are divorced.' He marries and divorces her twice. How many divorces have occurred?
A) 0 divorces
B) 1 divorce
C) 2 divorces
D) 3 divorces

Key Takeaway
Kullama triggers a fresh consequence with each occurrence of the condition, regardless of whether the object is specified or indefinite.

Intention in General Oaths: Diyanatan vs Qaḍā'an
Distinguishes 'kull' from 'kullama' and discusses intention validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between diyānatan and qaḍā'an regarding intention in oaths, and identify the tarjih between the majority and al-Khassaf's view.

Core Idea
Intention to specify a general oath is valid in conscience, not in court — except under duress.

In this lesson, we examine a key issue in Hanafi divorce law: when a man makes a general oath using 'kull' (every), does his private intention to limit it affect the ruling? The source distinguishes between religious validity (diyānatan) and judicial enforceability (qaḍā'an), and presents an intra-madhhab dispute between the majority and al-Khassaf. Understanding this distinction is crucial for judges and muftis handling such cases.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi majority position
Identify the ruling of the majority: intention to specify a general oath is valid only religiously (diyānatan), not judicially (qaḍā'an). The reason given is that 'takhṣīṣ al-ʿāmm khilāf al-ẓāhir' (specifying a general term contradicts the apparent meaning).
This is the Zahir al-Riwayah position.
2
Identify the opposing view within the school
Al-Khassaf holds that the intention is also valid judicially, especially when the swearer is oppressed. He argues that the oppressive situation provides a clear indication (dalāla ẓāhira) that the oath was limited.
Al-Khassaf is a notable Hanafi jurist; his view is recorded in al-Muhit and al-Walwaljiyya.
3
Follow the opposing proof
Al-Khassaf's dalil: when a person is forced to swear by a tyrant, the context (ḥāl) serves as a contextual clue that makes the intention apparent. Therefore, the intention should be accepted in court.
The text says 'li-anna al-ḥāla dalāla ẓāhira' (the situation is an apparent indication).
4
Analyse the Hanafi response and tarjih
The text does not refute al-Khassaf directly but allows adopting his view as a dispensation (rukhṣa) for the oppressed. However, the official fatwa (al-fatwā ʿalā ẓāhir al-madhhab) remains on the majority position.
The tarjih is based on the principle that apparent generality governs judicial decisions unless there is a clear contextual override.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under the majority view, a judge would enforce the general meaning of the oath (e.g., all future wives are divorced). Under al-Khassaf's view, the judge would accept the specific intention if the swearer is oppressed, thus not enforcing the divorce for those not intended.
This practical consequence matters for muftis advising individuals under duress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأفعال، ولو نوى بعض النساء صحت نيته ديانة لا قضاء لأن نية تخصيص العام خلاف الظاهر. وقال الخصاف : تصح نيته في القضاء أيضاً وهذا مخلص لمن يحلفه ظالم فأخذ بقوله لا بأس به لأن الحالة دلالة ظاهرة؛ كذا في المحيط والفتوى على ظاهر المذهب وإن أخذ بقول الخصاف إذا كان الحالف مظلوماً فلا بأس به كذا في الولوالجية.
...actions, and if he intended some women, his intention is valid religiously but not judicially, because the intention to specify a general term contradicts the apparent meaning. Al-Khassaf said: His intention is valid judicially as well, and this is a way out for one whom a tyrant makes swear. So if he adopts al-Khassaf's view, there is no harm because the situation is an apparent indication. This is in al-Muhit, and the fatwa is on the apparent school, though if the one swearing is oppressed and adopts al-Khassaf's view, there is no harm, as in al-Walwaljiyya.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the difference between 'kull' (every) and 'kullama' (whenever). The previous context explained that with 'kull', if a man says 'Every woman I marry is divorced,' and marries the same woman a second time, she is not divorced again, because 'kull' generalizes names, not actions. The author then addresses the issue of intention: if the man intended only specific women (e.g., only his current wives), his intention is valid in the eyes of Allah (diyanatan) but not in a judicial ruling (qaḍā'an), because specifying a general term opposite to its apparent meaning is not accepted in court. However, al-Khassaf (a Hanafi scholar) held that the intention is also valid judicially, especially for a person who is forced to swear by a tyrant. In such a case, his intention can be accepted as a dispensation because the situation provides a clear indication that he meant only those women. The text notes that the official fatwa is on the apparent position (that intention is not accepted in court), but if the swearer is oppressed, adopting al-Khassaf's view is permissible.

Key Terms
ديانة (diyānatan): Religiously or in conscience; the validity of an act in the sight of Allah, as opposed to judicial enforcement.
قضاء (qaḍā’): Judicially; the enforcement by a judge based on apparent evidence, not necessarily internal reality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the apparent meaning governs judicial rulings; intention affects only religious validity, except under duress where a dispensation exists.

Talaq Oaths with 'Kull' and 'Kullama'
Examples of talaq oaths with multiple wives and combined conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules of 'kull' and 'kullama' in talaq oaths to determine the number of divorces when multiple conditions are combined.

Core Idea
With 'kull', each wife is divorced once; with 'kullama', each act triggers a new divorce; conjunction with 'and' repeats both conditions.

In Hanafi fiqh, the precise wording of a divorce oath determines the outcome. This lesson examines three examples from Al-Bahr al-Raiq that illustrate how 'kull' (every) and 'kullama' (whenever) behave differently when combined with multiple wives and conditions. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly issuing and interpreting talaq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particle
Determine whether the wording uses 'kull' (general over subjects) or 'kullama' (repetition over actions).
2
Determine the scope of generalization
With 'kull', each subject (wife) is treated separately; the condition applies once per wife. With 'kullama', the action repeats each time it occurs.
3
Analyze conjunction (ʿaṭf)
If conditions are joined by 'and' under 'kullama', both conditions are repeated together. If a condition is separate with 'in', it does not repeat.
Conjunction shares the ruling of the first element.
4
Apply to each example
Work through the three examples: 'kull' with four wives, 'kullama' with one wife, and combined conditions with 'and' or 'in'.
5
Note the ikhtilaf
Consider Zufar's disagreement regarding the effect after remarriage and the final Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : لو كان له أربع نسوة فقال كل امرأة تدخل الدار فهي طالق فدخلت واحدة طلقت ولو دخلن طلقن، فإن دخلت تلك المرأة مرة أخرى لا تطلق . ولو قال كلما دخلت فدخلت امرأة طلقت ولو دخلت ثانياً تطلق وكذا ثالثاً، فإن تزوجت بعد الثلاث وعادت إلى الأول ثم دخلت لم تطلق خلافاً لزفر . ومنها لو قال كلما تزوجت امرأة ودخلت الدار فهي طالق فتزوج امرأة مرتين ثم دخلت الدار لم تطلق إلا مرة واحدة لأن قوله ودخلت عطف على التزوج وحكم المعطوف حكم المعوطف عليه وكلمة «كلما توجب التكرار فصار الدخول مكرراً أيضاً بخلاف ما لو قال كلما تزوجت امرأة فهي طالق إن دخلت الدار فتزوجها مراراً ودخلت مرة طلقت ثلاثاً لأنه لم يعطفه على الشرط المتكرر وإنما جعله شرطاً بـ (إن) وهي لا تفيد التكرار فصار الدخول شرط الحنث في الايمان كلها كذا في المحيط .
Among them: If he had four wives and said, 'Every woman who enters the house is divorced,' one entered - she is divorced; if all entered, all are divorced. If that woman enters again, she is not divorced. And if he said, 'Whenever I enter, a woman enters' - she is divorced; if she enters a second time, she is divorced, and similarly a third. Then if after the three (divorces) she marries another and returns to the first husband, then enters, she is not divorced, contrary to Zufar. Among them: If he said, 'Whenever I marry a woman and enter the house, she is divorced,' and he married a woman twice then entered the house, she is divorced only once. Because his statement 'and I enter' is conjoined to marriage, and the ruling of the conjoined is that of the conjoined to, and 'kullama' necessitates repetition, so entry also becomes repeated. Unlike if he said, 'Whenever I marry a woman, she is divorced if I enter the house,' and he married her multiple times and entered once, she is divorced three times. Because he did not conjoin it to the repeated condition but made it a condition with 'in', which does not indicate repetition, so entry becomes the condition of violation in all the oaths.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents three illustrative examples from Hanafi fiqh. The first example uses 'kull' (every woman) for four wives: if one enters, she is divorced once; if all enter, all are divorced. But if the same woman enters again after the first divorce, she is not divorced a second time because 'kull' generalizes over names, not actions, so each wife is divorced only once regardless of multiple entries. The second example uses 'kullama' for a single woman: if she enters, she is divorced; if she enters again, she is divorced again (second divorce), and a third time (third and final divorce). After three divorces, if she marries another and then returns to her first husband, she is not divorced upon re-entering because the previous husband has lost the right to divorce her (she is now a stranger unless she marries someone else and becomes divorced from him). Zufar disagreed, but the Hanafi position is as stated. The third example combines two conditions with 'and': 'Whenever I marry a woman and enter the house, she is divorced.' Here, if he marries the same woman twice but only enters once, only one divorce occurs. Since 'kullama' repeats both conditions (marriage and entry), each occurrence of both together is needed. However, if the entry is made a separate condition using 'in' (if), as in 'Whenever I marry a woman, she is divorced if I enter the house,' then each marriage is a separate oath, and a single entry triggers all the oaths, resulting in three divorces (if he married her three times).

Key Terms
عطف (ʿaṭf): Grammatical conjunction; when two conditions are joined by 'and' (waw), they both share the same ruling and are both subject to repetition if the particle is 'kullama'.
كل (kull): Every; used here to generalize over each wife individually, not over actions.
كلما (kullama): Whenever; indicates repetition of the action each time it occurs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says, 'Every woman who enters the kitchen is divorced.' He has three wives. One wife enters the kitchen three times. How many divorces occur?
A) One divorce
B) Three divorces
C) Nine divorces
D) No divorce

Key Takeaway
The particles 'kull' and 'kullama' have fundamentally different effects in conditional talaq: the former applies once per subject, the latter repeats per action.

Combining Divorce and Manumission with 'Kullama'
Explains the distinction between independent and dependent clauses in compound oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a man says 'Whenever I marry a woman, she is divorced and a slave of my slaves is free' and explain why the manumission occurs only once despite multiple marriages.

Core Idea
Independent clauses in an oath are not repeated; only dependent ones follow the condition.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario where a single oath combines two different legal consequences—divorce and manumission. The Hanafi jurists explain that the repetition of consequences depends on whether the clause is independent (ṣarīḥ) or dependent (kināyah). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying such conditional oaths in real-life situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure
Parse the oath: condition 'kullama tazawwajtu imra'ah' (whenever I marry a woman) with two consequences: 'hiya taliq' (she is divorced) and 'wa 'abd min 'abidi hurr' (and a slave of my slaves is free).
The word 'kullama' ties the condition to multiple occurrences.
2
Classify each consequence
Determine that 'hiya taliq' is kināyah (dependent, needing reference to the condition) while 'abd min 'abidi hurr' is ṣarīḥ (explicit and independent).
In Hanafi law, ṣarīḥ stands on its own; kināyah requires context from the condition.
3
Apply the principle
Apply the rule: independent clauses take their ruling from themselves and are not automatically repeated; dependent clauses are governed by the condition and repeat with each occurrence.
This prevents the obligation from becoming impossible or contradictory.
4
Predict outcome
First marriage: both consequences occur. Second marriage: only divorce occurs; no second slave is freed because the manumission clause is independent and not bound to repeat.
The slave is freed only once, as the clause is not designed for plural application.
5
Contrast with 'kull' and 'kullama'
Compare with a phrasing using 'kull' (every) vs. 'kullama'. With 'kull' and a singular indefinite, only one slave is freed. With 'kullama', if the manumission is directly tied to a physical act (like entry), it can repeat.
The author explains that 'kullama' generalizes the action, so each condition triggers a new consequence, provided the clause is dependent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها لو قال كلما تزوجت امرأة فهي طالق. وعبد من عبيدي حر فتزوج امرأة طلقت وعتق عبد من عبيده ، ولو تزوج أخرى طلقت ولا يعتق عبد من عبيده. وكذا ذكره الأسبيجابي. وأصله أن الكلام إذا كان تاماً مستقلاً بنفسه يؤخذ حكمه من نفسه لا من غيره، وإن كان ناقصاً غير مستقل بنفسه ولا مفهوم المعنى بذاته يؤخذ حكمه من غيره لئلا يلغو بنفسه والكناية لا تستقل بنفسها فأخذ حكمها من المكنى عنه والصريح معتبر بنفسه، فلو قال كل امرأة لي تدخل الدار فهي طالق وعبد من عبيدي حر فدخلن طلقن ولم يعتق إلا عبد واحد لأن العبد صريح مستقل بنفسه فلم ينعطف على الأول وأنه نكرة في الإثبات فيخص . ولو قال كلما والمسألة بحالها عتق أربعة عبيد لأن كلما أوجبت تعميم الفعل فصار كل دخول شرطاً على حدة وعتق العبد معلق بالدخول، ومن ضرورة تكرار الشرط تكرر الجزاء حتى يفيد، ومن ضرورة تكرار الجزاء تعميم الاسم.
Among them: If he said, 'Whenever I marry a woman, she is divorced and a slave of my slaves is free,' then he marries a woman: she is divorced and one of his slaves is freed. If he marries another, she is divorced but no slave of his is freed. Similarly mentioned by al-Asbijabi. The principle is that when the speech is complete and independent, its ruling is taken from itself, not from others. If it is incomplete, not independent, and its meaning cannot be understood by itself, its ruling is taken from another so that it is not futile. The kināyah (indirect expression) is not independent, so its ruling is taken from the referred object, but the explicit (ṣarīḥ) is considered by itself. Thus, if he said, 'Every woman of mine who enters the house is divorced, and a slave of my slaves is free,' they enter: they are divorced, but only one slave is freed. Because the slave is explicit and independent, so it is not conjoined to the first; and it is indefinite in positive context, so it is particularized. And if he said 'kullama' with the same case, four slaves are freed. Because 'kullama' necessitates generalization of the action, so each entry becomes a separate condition, and the freeing of the slave is suspended on entry. From the necessity of repeating the condition comes repeating the consequence so that it is effective, and from the necessity of repeating the consequence comes generalization of the name.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage examines a complex case where a single oath combines divorce and manumission. If one says 'Whenever I marry a woman, she is divorced and a slave of my slaves is free,' then upon the first marriage, both consequences occur. However, upon a second marriage, only the divorce occurs, not a second manumission. The reason is that the phrase 'a slave of my slaves is free' is an independent, self-contained statement (ṣarīḥ). It is not dependent on the condition expressed by 'kullama' in the way the divorce is. The divorce is understood as a direct consequence of the condition (each marriage triggers it), but the manumission is an additional statement that is not repeated because it is not linked to the repetition of the condition. The author explains a general principle: an independent clause takes its ruling from itself; a dependent clause (like a pronoun or kināyah) takes its ruling from the antecedent. The divorce is the primary consequence; the manumission is a separate, explicit statement that is not automatically repeated. However, if the oath uses 'kullama' and the manumission is directly tied to the entry (as in the later example where the manumission is obviously linked to the physical act of entry), then it does repeat. The passage contrasts two scenarios: with 'kull' (every) and with 'kullama'. With 'kull', only one slave is freed regardless of multiple entries. With 'kullama', each entry frees a separate slave because the action is generalized and each entry is a separate condition.

Key Terms
كناية (kināyah): An indirect or ambiguous expression; in this context, the divorce phrase 'she is divorced' is treated as a kināyah that requires specification from the condition, whereas 'a slave is free' is ṣarīḥ (explicit) and self-standing.
صريح (ṣarīḥ): Explicit, unambiguous; a statement that conveys its meaning independently without needing reference to another part of the sentence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Two slaves
B) One slave
C) No slave
D) Depends on his intention

Key Takeaway
Independent clauses in a conditional oath are not automatically repeated with each occurrence of the condition; only dependent clauses linked to the condition are.

Applying 'Kull' vs 'Kullama' to Slaves, Daughters, and Houses
Examples distinguishing 'kull' and 'kullama'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that 'kull' generalizes names but not actions, and indefinite nouns in positive context remain singular, to determine the number of emancipation or obligations in statements.

Core Idea
'Kull' collects names, not actions; indefinite nouns in positive context stay singular.

In this lesson, we explore several examples from Al-Bahr al-Raiq that illustrate the critical distinction between 'kull' (every) and 'kullama' (whenever). The key difference is that 'kull' generalizes nouns (e.g., houses) but does not repeat actions (e.g., entering), while 'kullama' suspends a condition on repeated events. Understanding this nuance prevents errors in counting divorces, emancipations, and obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of noun after 'kull'
Determine whether the noun following 'kull' is definite or indefinite. Indefinite nouns (e.g., 'a slave', 'a hajj') in a positive statement are not automatically generalized unless the wording requires it.
In the example 'Every house I enter, I owe a hajj', 'hajj' is indefinite, so it refers to a single hajj.
2
Check whether the verb is repeated
The verb (e.g., 'enter') is not repeated by 'kull' because 'kull' applies to names, not actions. The condition is the mere occurrence of entry, not each entry.
Thus, if he enters multiple houses, the condition is fulfilled once, and only one hajj is due.
3
Compare with 'kullama' phrasing
If the wording uses 'kullama' (whenever) or a similar repeated condition, each occurrence triggers the consequence. 'Kullama' explicitly repeats the condition.
For example, 'Whenever I marry you' with 'kullama' would cause divorce each time marriage occurs.
4
Apply to the slave girl example
In 'Every slave-girl who enters is free, and her child, and a slave of my slaves is free': all slave-girls and their children are freed, but only one male slave is freed because 'a slave' is indefinite.
The children are attached to the mothers, so each child is freed alongside its mother.
5
Handle specific persons and re-marriage
If one says 'Every woman I marry' (without 'kullama') and later marries the same woman again, she is divorced only once. But if specified with 'kullama', it repeats.
The key is whether the wording emphasizes the act of marrying or the identity of the woman.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال كل جارية تدخل فهي حرة وولدها وعبد من عبيدي حر فدخلن جميعاً عتقن وعتق الأولاد كلهم ولم يعتق إلا عبد واحد . ولو قال كل دار دخلتها فعلي حجة فدخل دوراً لم يلزمه إلا حجة لأنه صرح بالحجة وهي نكرة في الإثبات فتخص ولم يقترن بها ما يوجب تعميمها ولم يعلقها بشرط مكرر فإن الدخول غير مكرر لأن كلمة «كل» تجمع الأسماء دون الأفعال. ولو قال فعلي بها حجة لزمه بكل دار حجة، وتمامه في المحيط إلا أنه يشكل بفرع الأسبيجابي ولعل الصواب في عبارة الأسبيجابي كل امرأة أتزوجها دون «كلما» كما لا يخفى . فإن تزوجها ثانياً لا تطلق إلا مرة واحدة ولو قال ذلك لمعينة كلما تزوجتك أو تزوجت فلانة تكرر دائماً .
And if he said, 'Every slave-girl who enters is free, and her child, and a slave of my slaves is free,' and they all entered, they are freed and all the children are freed, but only one (male) slave is freed. And if he said, 'Every house I enter, I owe a hajj,' and he entered houses, only one hajj is due. Because he explicitly mentioned 'hajj', which is indefinite in positive context and thus becomes particularized; nothing necessitates its generalization, and he did not suspend it on a repeated condition, for entry is not repeated because 'kull' collects names, not actions. But if he said, 'I owe her a hajj,' then he owes a hajj for each house. The full discussion is in al-Muhit, except that it is problematic regarding the branch of al-Asbijabi; probably the correct view is that al-Asbijabi's phrase is 'every woman I marry' without 'kullama', as is not hidden. Then if he marries her a second time, she is divorced only once. And if he said that for a specific woman, 'Whenever I marry you' or 'Whenever I marry so-and-so,' it repeats always.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage continues with more examples illustrating the distinction between 'kull' and 'kullama'. In the first example, one says 'Every slave-girl who enters is free, and her child, and a slave of my slaves is free.' When all slave-girls enter, each is freed, and each of their children is freed, but only one male slave is freed (even if multiple are owned). This is because 'a slave of my slaves' is an indefinite noun in a positive context, which by default indicates singularity (one slave). The children, however, are attached to their mothers, so each mother's child is freed with her. The second example: if one says 'Every house I enter, I owe a hajj' and enters multiple houses, only one hajj is due because 'hajj' is indefinite in a positive statement, so it refers to a single hajj. Also, 'kull' deals with names (houses) not actions, so the action of entering is not repeated; the condition is the essence of entry, not multiple entries. However, if he says 'I owe her a hajj' (using 'her' to refer to the house), then he owes a separate hajj for each house because the pronoun 'her' indicates a particular hajj for each house. The author notes that al-Asbijabi's formulation should be 'every woman I marry' without 'kullama' to avoid confusion. Finally, a summary: if one says 'Every woman I marry' (with 'kull') and marries the same woman a second time, she is divorced only once. But if one specifies a particular woman and says 'Whenever I marry you' (with 'kullama'), it repeats with each marriage.

Key Terms
نكرة في الإثبات (nakira fī l-ithbāt): An indefinite noun in a positive (affirmative) context; according to Hanafi usul, it carries a general meaning but can be particularized when used with 'kull'.
كل (kull): Every; a word that generalizes nouns (names) but does not generalize actions. In conditional sentences, it does not repeat the condition.
كلما (kullama): Whenever; a word that makes the condition repeat with each occurrence of the verb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says, 'Every house I enter, I owe a hajj,' and enters three houses, how many hajj is he required to perform?
A) Three
B) One
C) Zero
D) Depends on the houses

Key Takeaway
'Kull' generalizes names but not actions; indefinite nouns in positive context remain singular unless particularized by a pronoun or context.

10 min
Conditional Divorce with 'Kullamā': Entry and Multiple Wives
Ruling on conditional divorce with 'kullamā' involving house entry and multiple wives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for each phrasing of conditional divorce with 'kullamā' involving entry and apply the distinction between distributed and cumulative results.

Core Idea
With 'kullamā', each occurrence of the condition triggers a separate result unless the phrasing ties the result to a cumulative event.

This passage from al-Muhit presents four key scenarios on conditional divorce using 'kullamā' (whenever). The rulings hinge on how the condition and result are connected – whether each instance of the condition produces a separate divorce, or the result is deferred until a later cumulative trigger. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly applying conditional divorce in complex family situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key particle and its implication
Recognize that 'kullamā' means 'whenever' and implies repetition: each time the condition occurs, the result is repeated. However, the specific phrasing may modify this general rule.
Contrast with 'idhā' (if) which does not imply repetition.
2
Analyze the first scenario: multiple wives with unspecified entry
The man says 'Whenever I enter this house, my wife is divorced' and has four wives. He enters four times without specifying a wife. Each entry causes one divorce. The divorces can be distributed among wives or all accumulated on one.
The total number of divorces equals the number of entries (four).
3
Analyze the second scenario: condition of entry and speaking
He says 'Whenever I enter this house and speak to so-and-so, one of my slaves is free.' He enters and speaks once. Only one slave is freed because the speaking is a single act – the repetition of the condition (entry) does not multiply the result since the result is tied to the combined act.
If he entered multiple times without speaking, no slave is freed until he speaks.
4
Analyze the third scenario: nested condition with entry then speech
He says 'Whenever I enter this house, if I speak to so-and-so, you are divorced.' He enters three times without speaking, then speaks. Three divorces occur because each entry had a pending condition that was fulfilled when he spoke.
The speech fulfills the condition for all previous entries that included the conditional speech clause.
5
Analyze the fourth scenario: double 'kullamā' structure
He says 'Whenever I enter this house, whenever I speak to so-and-so, you are divorced.' The second 'kullamā' clause is suspended on entry. Entering activates it, and then each subsequent speaking triggers a separate divorce (up to three).
The entry is a necessary precondition for the second oath to take effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها ما لو قال كلما دخلت هذه الدار فأمرأتي طالق وله أربع نسوة فدخلها أربع مرات ولم يعين واحدة منهن بعينها يقع بكل دخلة واحدة إن شاء فرقها عليهن وإن شاء جمعها على واحدة . ولو قال كلما دخلت هذه الدار وكلمت فلاناً أو فكلمت فلاناً فعبد من عبيدي حر مراراً وكلمت مرة لم يعتق إلا عبد واحد. ولو قال كلما دخلت هذه الدار فإن كلمت فلاناً فأنت طالق فدخلت ثلاثاً ثم كلمت فلاناً طلقت ثلاثاً، ولو قال كلما دخلت هذه الذار فكلما كلمت فلاناً فأنت طالق فاليمين الثانية تصير معلقة بالدخول وإذا دخلت الدار انعقدت اليمين الثانية فإذا كلمت فلاناً ثلاث مرات بعد ذلك طلقت ثلاثاً؛ كذا في المحيط.
And among them is what if he said, 'Whenever I enter this house, my wife is divorced,' and he has four wives, and he entered it four times without specifying one of them specifically, one divorce occurs with each entry; if he wishes, he can distribute them among them, and if he wishes, he can accumulate them on one. And if he said, 'Whenever I enter this house and speak to so-and-so,' or 'or speak to so-and-so,' then a slave among my slaves is freed repeatedly, and he spoke once, only one slave is freed. And if he said, 'Whenever I enter this house, if I speak to so-and-so, then you are divorced,' and he entered three times then spoke to so-and-so, she is divorced three times. And if he said, 'Whenever I enter this house, whenever I speak to so-and-so, you are divorced,' the second oath becomes suspended on the entry; when he enters the house, the second oath becomes contracted, so if he speaks to so-and-so three times after that, she is divorced three times; thus in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage from al-Muhit presents several rulings on conditional divorce using 'kullamā' with house entry. In the first scenario, a man has four wives and says 'Whenever I enter this house, my wife is divorced.' He enters four times. Since he did not specify which wife, each entry causes one divorce, which he can either distribute among the wives (e.g., one each) or accumulate all on one wife. In the second scenario, he conditions freeing a slave on entry and speaking; if he speaks once, only one slave is freed because 'kullamā' implies repetition but here the condition is a single act (speaking once). In the third, he says 'Whenever I enter, if I speak, you are divorced'; entering three times without speaking does not trigger the divorce until he speaks, then all three divorces occur because each entry had the potential. In the fourth, he says 'Whenever I enter, whenever I speak'; the second clause is suspended on entry, so entering contracts the second oath, and then each speaking causes a divorce up to three.

Key Terms
كلما (kullamā): Conditional particle meaning 'whenever', imposing repetition of both condition and result.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man with four wives says 'Whenever I enter this house, my wife is divorced.' He enters four times without specifying a wife. How many divorces occur in total?
A) One
B) Four
C) Sixteen
D) Depends on the judge

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on 'kullamā' conditional divorces depends on the precise wording: 'kullamā' generally repeats the result with each occurrence, but nested or combined conditions can limit repetition.

The Dispute Regarding the Word 'Kullama' in Conditional Divorce
Explains the two riwayat on oath contracted with 'kullama' and its practical consequence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi riwayat on the effect of 'kullama' in conditional divorce and identify the mufta bihi position with its justification.

Core Idea
The fatwa is on al-Jami': 'kullama' creates multiple oaths at once—precaution overrides textual reading.

In the Hanafi school, even within Zahir al-Riwayah, there are differing transmissions on how the word 'kullama' (whenever) functions in a conditional oath of divorce. This lesson unpacks the two riwayat from Imam Muhammad's works and explains why the cautious view of al-Jami' is preferred for fatwa. You will see how a subtle difference in linguistic interpretation leads to radically different practical outcomes.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (mufta bihi)
According to the riwaya of al-Jami', 'kullama' creates multiple independent oaths simultaneously at the moment of utterance. This is the relied-upon position for fatwa.
The fatwa is on al-Jami' because it is ahwat (more precautionary).
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi variant
The riwaya of al-Mabsut holds that only one oath is contracted, which then renews each time the condition occurs again.
This variant is also from Zahir al-Riwayah but is not the mufta bihi.
3
Follow the proof for each view
Al-Jami' treats 'kullama' as equivalent to repeating the condition and result. Al-Mabsut argues that since the result (jaza') is mentioned only once, only one original oath exists.
Neither view cites external dalil; the dispute is purely linguistic within the school.
4
Analyse why al-Jami' is preferred
The Hanafi school gives precedence to the view that is more precautionary (ahwat) in matters of divorce, as it protects against inadvertently exceeding the number of permissible divorces.
Tarjih mechanism: al-fatwa 'ala riwayat al-jami' li-annahu ahwat.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if a man says 'Whenever I swear, you are divorced' and then swears with 'kullama', al-Jami' says three divorces occur immediately; al-Mabsut says only one.
This difference has real consequences for the validity of later remarriages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب التعليق ا هـ. يوجد فطلقها واحدة طلقت ثلاثاً ا هـ . ومنها ما في المحيط : ثم المنعقد بكلمة «كلما» يمين واحدة. للحال ويتجدد انعقادها مرة بعد أخرى كلما حنث في يمينه أما أيمان منعقدة على رواية الجامع أيمان منعقدة للحال انحلت بعضها وبقي بعضها منعقدة بعد الحنث إلى أن شرطها، وعلى رواية المبسوط المنعقدة للحال يمين واحدة ويتجدد انعقادها مرة بعد أخرى كلما حنث لأن الجزاء لم يذكر إلا مرة وهو المعتبر وجه رواية الجامع أن «كلما» بمنزلة تكرار الشرط والجزاء والفتوى على رواية الجامع لأنه أحوط ا ولم يذكر ثمرة الاختلاف، وينبغي أن تظهر الثمرة فيما إذا حلف بالطلاق لا يحلف بأن قال كلما حلفت فأنت طالق ثم علق بكلمة «كلما»، فعلى رواية الجامع يقع الآن الثلاث، وعلى رواية المبسوط يقع الآن واحدة. وأما إذا حلف بالله أن لا يحلف فينبغي أن تجب كفارة واحدة للحال اتفاقاً لأنه لا يعلم ما زاد على اليمين الواحدة. وفي البزازية من كتاب القضاء : لو قال لامرأة كلما تزوجتك فأنت طالق ثلاثاً ثم تزوجها ورفع الحال إلى حاكم يرى صحة النكاح فقضى بها ثم طلقها ثلاثاً ثم تزوجها بعد دخول زوج آخر اختلف المشايخ في أنه هل يحتاج إلى القضاء ثانياً بناء على أن المنعقدة بكلمة «كلما للحال يمين واحدة يتجدد انعقادها كلما وقع الحنث وهو رواية الأصل، أم المنعقدة بها في الحال أيمان كما هو رواية الجامع وهو الأصح فيحنث في البعض لوجود الشرط وتبقى الباقية منعقدة؟ فمن قال بهذا شرط القضاء ثانياً . ومن قال بالأول لم يشترط القضاء ثانياً ا هـ . وهذا بيان ثمرة الاختلاف في المعلق بالتزوج لا مطلقاً.
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Conditional Divorce. It exists: if he divorced her with one divorce, she is divorced with three. Among them is what is in al-Muhit: The oath contracted with the word 'kullama' is one oath for the present, and its contract renews time after time every time he breaks his oath. As for oaths contracted according to the riwaya of al-Jami', they are oaths contracted for the present, some of them are dissolved and some remain contracted after the breaking (of the oath) until its condition. According to the riwaya of al-Mabsut, the contracted (oath) for the present is one oath, and its contract renews time after time every time he breaks (the oath) because the result (jaza') is not mentioned except once, and that is what is considered. The justification for the riwaya of al-Jami' is that 'kullama' is like repeating the condition and the result. The fatwa is on the riwaya of al-Jami' because it is more precautionary. The fruit of the difference is not mentioned. And it is appropriate that the fruit appears in the case where he swears by divorce: 'I do not swear', saying: 'Whenever I swear, you are divorced' then he conditions with the word 'kullama'. According to the riwaya of al-Jami', three (divorces) occur now, and according to the riwaya of al-Mabsut, one occurs now. As for if he swears by Allah not to swear, then it is appropriate that one expiation is obligatory for the present by agreement, because he does not know what is more than one oath. And in al-Bazzaziyya from the Book of Judgments: If a man said to a woman: 'Whenever I marry you, you are divorced thrice,' then he married her and raised the case to a judge who considers the marriage valid, and he judged accordingly, then he divorced her thrice, then he married her after the entry of another husband, the mashayikh differed on whether he needs a second judgment, based on whether the (oath) contracted with the word 'kullama' is one oath for the present that renews its contract every time the breaking occurs, which is the riwaya of the original, or whether the contracted (oaths) with it are multiple oaths for the present, as is the riwaya of al-Jami', which is the most sound, so he breaks some due to the existence of the condition, and the remaining remain contracted? Whoever said this requires a second judgment. Whoever said the first did not require a second judgment. This is an explanation of the fruit of the difference in the case of conditioning by marriage, not absolutely.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the legal effect of the word 'kullama' (whenever) in conditional divorce. The author presents two riwayat (transmitted views) from Imam Muhammad's works: (1) The riwaya of al-Jami' holds that 'kullama' creates multiple independent oaths simultaneously at the moment of utterance. Thus, when the condition occurs, all of them are broken at once. (2) The riwaya of al-Mabsut holds that 'kullama' creates only one oath for the present, which then renews each time the condition occurs again. The passage explains the justification for each view: in al-Jami', 'kullama' is treated as repeating both the condition and the result; in al-Mabsut, the result (jaza') is mentioned only once, so only one original oath exists. The fatwa is given on the riwaya of al-Jami' because it is more precautionary (ahwat). The practical consequence (thamarah) is illustrated with two cases: (a) If a man says, 'Whenever I swear, you are divorced,' and then conditions with 'kullama,' according to al-Jami', three divorces occur immediately; according to al-Mabsut, only one. (b) If a man swears by Allah not to swear, only one expiation is due because the number of oaths is unknown. A further example from al-Bazzaziyya involves a conditional marriage divorce, where the need for a second judicial ruling depends on which riwaya is followed. The dispute is strictly limited to conditioning with marriage, not absolutely.

Key Terms
كلما (kullama): A conditional particle meaning 'whenever'; used to link a condition to a result that repeats with each occurrence of the condition.
رواية الجامع (riwayat al-jāmi‘): The transmitted ruling from Imam Muhammad's book al-Jami', which holds that 'kullama' creates multiple oaths simultaneously.
ثمرة الاختلاف (thamaratu al-ikhtilāf): The practical legal consequence that results from a difference of opinion within the school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says to his wife, 'Whenever I swear, you are divorced,' and then he conditions with the word 'kullama,' how many divorces occur immediately according to the riwaya of al-Jami'?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
The fatwa is on the riwaya of al-Jami': 'kullama' creates multiple oaths at once, because it is more precautionary, even though the wording might suggest a single repeated oath.

Foundations: Loss of Ownership and Imkān al-Birr in Conditional Oaths
Introduces the core rules on ownership and possibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that loss of ownership (less than three divorces) does not invalidate a conditional oath, and explain that imkān al-birr is a necessary condition for the validity and continuation of such oaths.

Core Idea
Loss of ownership does not break a conditional oath unless it is total; loss of the ability to fulfill does.

In this lesson, we examine the foundational principles governing conditional oaths (ta'liq). The passage explains two key factors: the effect of losing ownership (zawāl al-milk) and the necessity of maintaining the possibility of fulfilling the condition (imkān al-birr). We will see that as long as the condition remains possible, the oath endures even if ownership is partially lost, but if the ability to fulfill vanishes, the oath is invalidated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two factors affecting validity
Recognize that the passage discusses loss of ownership and loss of imkān al-birr as separate issues affecting the oath.
Loss of ownership applies to marriage and slavery; loss of imkān al-birr applies to the sworn act.
2
Analyze the rule on loss of ownership
Understand that if ownership ends by less than three divorces, the oath remains because the subject (marriage) can be restored. If by three, the subject is permanently removed, invalidating the oath.
The passage says 'loss of ownership after the oath does not invalidate it' and later clarifies that three divorces is an exception.
3
Understand imkān al-birr as a condition
Recognize that the ability to perform the sworn act is a condition for the validity (initial contract) and also for the survival (continuation) of time-bound oaths.
Rational possibility suffices for validity, but if that possibility ceases before the deadline, the oath is invalidated.
4
Apply to the first examples
Work through the examples of debt discharge and garment return: if the creditor forgives the debt within the period, the oath is invalid because the condition becomes impossible; if the garment is taken by the husband himself, he does not break his oath.
In the garment case, the wife had control, but the husband preempted her action – she is not at fault.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Observe that Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that time-bound oaths become invalid if the act becomes impossible, while Abu Yusuf holds that hanth occurs if the act was rationally possible at the time of swearing.
This dispute recurs in the furu' cases in the second lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

واحدة أو ثنتين فانقضت عدتها ثم تزوجها ثم وجد الشرط طلقت. أطلق الملك فشمل ملك النكاح وملك اليمين حتى لو قال لعبده إذا دخلت الدار فباعه ثم اشتراه فدخل عتق . وقيد بزوال الملك لأن زوال إمكان البر المصحح
