بالعتق وهو عدم الدخول حتى لو كانت اليمين الأولى بعتق أو طلاق حنث في اليمينين لأن لها مدخلاً في القضاء. ولو ادعى على رجل ديناً فحلف المدعى عليه بالطلاق ما له عليه شيء فأقام المدعي البينة وقضى به له ينظر ؛ إن قال كان له علي دين وأوفيته لم تطلق امرأته، وإن قال لم يكن له على شيء قط طلقت امرأته وتمامه فيه.
His saying: (So if the condition occurs in ownership, she is divorced and the oath dissolves) because the condition has occurred and the subject is capable of the consequence, so it takes effect, and the oath does not remain because its remaining is dependent on the remaining of the condition and the consequence, and neither remains. And in al-Qunyah: He said to her: 'If you leave the house without my permission, you are divorced.' Then a flood or overwhelming fire occurred, and she left; she does not break the oath. End quote. Even though the condition occurred, but the condition is leaving without his permission for something other than flood or fire. And in it, near the section on maintenance: He said to his slave wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced three times,' then her master freed her, and she entered; two divorces occur. And in Jami' al-Karkhi: Two divorces occur, and the husband has the right of return. He has a wife who is junub, menstruating, and postpartum; he said: 'The most impure of you is divorced,' the postpartum woman is divorced. And in 'the most obscene of you' applies to the menstruating woman because it is textual. End quote. He speaks generally about ownership, so it applies whether the condition occurs during the waiting period as we mentioned before the chapter of tafwid. He does not mean that the entire condition must occur in ownership, but rather its completion must be in ownership. So if he says to her: 'If you menstruate twice, you are divorced,' and she menstruates the first time outside his ownership and the second time in his ownership, she is divorced. Similarly, if he marries her an hour before she becomes pure from the second menstruation, or after the blood stops but before she bathes and her days are less than ten, then when she bathes or the time for prayer passes, she is divorced, because the condition has been completed while she is in his marriage. Likewise, if he says: 'If you eat this loaf, you are divorced,' and she eats most of the loaf outside his ownership, then he marries her and she eats the remainder, she is divorced, because the condition is completed in his ownership and the breaking of the oath occurs by it. This is in al-Mabsut. And he will explicitly state that ownership is required for the last of the two conditions, and our discussion here is about a single condition. In al-Bazzaziyya: 'You are divorced if you do such-and-such' – she is not divorced unless everything occurs, even if he repeats the conditional particle. If he says: 'If you eat or drink' and he presents the consequence first, then whatever occurs from her, the divorce occurs and the oath is lifted. But if he delays the divorce, it does not occur until all matters occur according to Muhammad. According to Abu Yusuf, if one occurs, the divorce occurs and the oath is lifted. End quote. And what is appropriate to his saying 'if the condition occurs, she is divorced' is what is in al-Muhit from the chapter on oaths that contradict each other: If the defendant swears by divorce saying 'My wife is divorced if you have a thousand against me,' and the plaintiff provides evidence and judgment is given, the one who swore breaks his oath according to Abu Yusuf, and it is a narration from Muhammad, and from him that he does not break. If he provides evidence of the plaintiff's acknowledgment of a thousand, it is mentioned in Waqi'at al-Natifi that he does not break. If two men have a house in their hands and each swears that the house is his, and they provide evidence, it is between them and they both break their oath. If it is in the hand of one of them, the one in possession breaks his oath because the external evidence is given precedence over him. If someone swears by Allah that he did not enter this house today, then says: 'My slave is free if he did not enter it today,' there is no expiation and his slave is not freed, because if he is truthful in the oath by Allah, he does not break it and there is no expiation; if he is lying, it is an oath of ghammus, so expiation is not obligatory. The oath by Allah has no bearing in judiciary, so he is not considered a liar legally, so the condition of breaking the oath by emancipation (non-entry) is not realized. If the first oath were by emancipation or divorce, he would break both oaths because they have a bearing in judiciary. And if someone claims a debt from a man, and the defendant swears by divorce saying 'Nothing is owed to him,' then the plaintiff brings evidence and judgment is given for him, it is considered: if he says 'He owed me a debt and I paid it,' his wife is not divorced; if he says 'He never owed me anything,' his wife is divorced. Its completion is in it.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling that for a conditional divorce to take effect, the condition must occur while the wife is still in the husband's ownership (milk). The text explains the general rule and then provides various examples from al-Qunyah, Jami' al-Karkhi, al-Bazzaziyya, and al-Muhit. The key principle is that the completion of the condition (tamam al-shart) must occur in the marriage. For instance, if the condition is menstruating twice, and the first menstruation occurs before marriage but the second after marriage, the divorce occurs because the condition is completed in ownership. Similarly, eating part of a loaf before marriage and the rest after marriage results in divorce. The text also discusses the difference between single conditions and multiple conditions, and the disagreement between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding whether partial fulfillment of multiple conditions triggers divorce. The passage then moves to related examples from oaths that contradict each other, illustrating how the courts handle such cases.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A condition upon which the divorce is made contingent; the divorce only occurs if the condition is fulfilled.
ملك (milk): Ownership, specifically here the marital bond (nikah) that gives the husband ownership of the wife's obedience and the right to divorce.
يمين (yamin): An oath, here specifically a conditional divorce is considered a type of oath (yamin bi al-talaq).
تمام الشرط (tamam al-shart): The completion of the condition; it is this completion that must occur while the wife is in the husband's ownership.
تقديم الجزاء (taqdim al-jaza): Presenting the consequence (divorce) before mentioning the conditions; this affects the ruling on multiple conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says to his wife 'If you eat this loaf, you are divorced,' and she eats half of the loaf before marriage and the other half after marriage, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) She is not divorced because the condition was not fully fulfilled in marriage.
B) She is divorced because the condition was completed in marriage.
C) She is divorced only if she ate the entire loaf after marriage.
D) It depends on her intention.

Key Takeaway
For a conditional divorce to take effect, the condition must be completed while the wife is in the husband's marriage; partial occurrence outside the marriage does not prevent the divorce from occurring.

Interpreting Oaths by Meaning: Context and Custom Override Literalism
Examples of conditional oaths interpreted according to custom and context rather than literal wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that conditional oaths are interpreted by intended meaning and custom, using the examples from al-Qunyah to determine when an oath is not broken.

Core Idea
An oath is judged by its intended meaning and customary context, not by literal wording.

In Hanafi fiqh, oaths are not always interpreted literally. When a person's words carry a customary or contextual meaning, that meaning takes precedence. This lesson examines four complete examples from al-Qunyah, cited in al-Bahr al-Raiq, where a conditional oath is not broken because the literal wording would contradict the intended sense or common practice. Understanding these examples will help you apply the same principle in analogous cases.

Guided Path
1
Extract the Principle
Read the opening sentence: 'ثم اعلم أن ها هنا مسائل في الإيمان تحمل على المعنى دون ظاهر اللفظ'. This sets the rule: oaths are interpreted by meaning, not literal wording. Identify this as the governing principle.
The principle is a sub-maxim of the broader Hanafi approach to oaths.
2
Analyze Each Example
For each example, contrast the literal wording with the intended meaning based on context, custom, or situation. For instance, the drunk person's claim of being a slave is understood as humility, not a factual statement.
Pay attention to the specific context that shifts interpretation.
3
Determine the Outcome
In each case, the outcome is that the oath is not broken (لا يحنث). Verify that the intended meaning overrides the literal trigger.
The ruling is consistent: no breach, no expiation required.
4
Identify the Underlying Custom
Find the common thread: 'urf (custom) or necessity causes the interpretation. Forgetfulness is excused, giving through a middleman counts as giving, and touching a spindle without spinning is not the intended action.
This aligns with the Hanafi reliance on 'urf in legal interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن ها هنا مسائل في الإيمان تحمل على المعنى دون ظاهر اللفظ منها : لو قال سكران لآخر إن لم أكن عبداً لك فامرأته طالق ثلاثاً لا يحنث إن كان متواضعاً له. ومنها إن وضعت يدك على المغزل فكذا فوضعت يدها عليه ولم تغزل لا يحنث ومنها إن دفعت لأخيك شيئاً ودفع إليها أرزاً ليدفع إليه لا يحنث ومنها خرج من داره وحلف لا يرجع ثم رجع لشيء نسيه في داره لا يحنث ؛ كذا في القنية وفيها لو قال لامرأتين له أطولكما حياة طالق لا تطلق في الحال، فلو كانت إحداهما بنت ستين سنة والأخرى بنت عشرين سنة فماتت العجوز قبل
Then know that here there are issues regarding oaths that are interpreted according to meaning, not the literal wording. Among them: If a drunk person says to another: 'If I am not your slave, my wife is thrice divorced,' he does not break his oath if he is humble towards him. Another: If she says: 'If you put your hand on the spindle, then such-and-such,' and she put her hand on it but did not spin, he does not break his oath. Another: If she said: 'If you give something to your brother,' and she gave rice to him to give to him, she does not break the oath. Another: He left his house and swore not to return, then returned for something he forgot in his house, he does not break the oath. This is in al-Qunyah. In it: If he said to his two wives: 'The one of you who lives longer is divorced,' she is not divorced immediately. So if one of them is a sixty-year-old and the other is a twenty-year-old, and the old woman dies before
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage lists several examples from al-Qunyah where conditional oaths (including those involving divorce or emancipation) are interpreted according to meaning (al-ma'na) rather than literal wording (zahir al-lafz). The principle is that oaths are understood based on the intention and context, especially when strict literalism would lead to unjust or unintended results. For instance, a drunk person's statement that he is a slave is understood as an expression of humility, not a factual claim. Similarly, an oath about using a spindle is about the action of spinning, not just touching. Giving rice to a brother through his wife is considered giving. Forgetting something after swearing not to return is excused because the oath is understood to exclude forgetfulness. The passage ends mid-example about which wife dies first.

Key Terms
الأيمان تحمل على المعنى (al-ayman tuhmal 'ala al-ma'na): The principle that oaths are interpreted according to their intended meaning and custom, not just literal wording.
سكران (sakran): A person who is drunk; his statements are not legally binding if he lacks intent, but here his statement is interpreted metaphorically.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh interprets conditional oaths by their intended meaning and customary context, not literal wording, as shown in examples from al-Qunyah.

Conditional Divorce Spanning Before and After Marriage
Ruling on conditional divorce when condition spans marriage boundary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a condition for divorce need only be completed within the husband's ownership (marriage) for the divorce to take effect, even if part of the condition occurred before marriage.

Core Idea
A condition for divorce is completed at its final moment; if that moment occurs within marriage, the divorce takes effect.

In Hanafi fiqh, conditional divorce (talaq mu'allaq) is suspended on the occurrence of a condition. A nuanced scenario arises when the condition begins before the marriage contract is executed. This lesson examines the ruling from al-Mabsut as commented upon by Ibn Nujaym, showing that only the completion of the condition—not its initiation—determines the legal effect. Understanding this principle prevents confusion about whether pre-marriage acts can trigger divorce.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the issue
Identify the specific case: a man says to a woman (not his wife) 'If you eat this loaf, you are divorced.' She eats most of it before marriage, then after marriage she eats the remainder. The question is whether the divorce occurs.
The condition is a single act (eating the loaf), not multiple conditions.
2
Identify the key principle
The ruling hinges on the completion of the condition. The passage states: 'because the condition was completed in his ownership and the breaking of the oath occurs by that.' Ownership here refers to the marriage bond.
Only the final moment of the condition matters, not the beginning.
3
Apply the principle to the example
Since she ate the remainder after marriage, the condition was fully completed while she was his wife. Thus the divorce takes effect. The pre-marriage portion does not prevent this.
This is analogous to the earlier examples of bathing or prayer time.
4
Note the distinction from other cases
The author hints that for multiple conditions (e.g., two separate conditions), ownership is required for the last condition only. Here we have a single condition, so the rule is straightforward.
This distinction will be clarified later in the text.
5
Conclude with the Hanafi fatwa
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the divorce occurs. This ruling from al-Mabsut is part of Zahir al-Riwayah and is authoritative.
No intra-Hanafi disagreement is noted in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اغتسلت أو مضى عليها وقت صلاة طلقت لأن الشرط قد تم وهي في نكاحه، وكذا لو قال إن أكلت هذا الرغيف فأنت طالق فأكلت عامة الرغيف في غير ملكه ثم تزوجها فأكلت ما بقي منه طلقت لأن الشرط تم في ملكه والحنث به يحصل ؛ كذا في المبسوط. وسيصرح بأن الملك يشترط لآخر الشرطين وكلامنا هنا في الشرط الواحد
If she bathes or a prayer time passes, she is divorced because the condition has been fulfilled while she is in his marriage; similarly, if he said, 'If you eat this loaf, you are divorced,' and she ate most of the loaf while not in his ownership, then he married her and she ate the remainder, she is divorced because the condition was completed in his ownership and breaking the oath occurs by that; as in al-Mabsut. And he will explicitly state that ownership is required for the last of two conditions, and our discussion here is about a single condition.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from al-Mabsut on conditional divorce when the condition spans both before and after marriage. The first example: if he says 'if you bathe' or 'if a prayer time passes' while she is a wife, she is divorced upon occurrence. But here the author gives a more complex case: a man says to a woman not yet his wife, 'If you eat this loaf, you are divorced.' She eats most of it outside his ownership. Then he marries her, and she eats the remainder. The ruling is that the divorce occurs because the condition was completed within his ownership (the last part). The key principle: for a single condition, only the completion of the condition within the husband's ownership matters. But the author notes that for multiple conditions, ownership is required for the last part only, a point to be discussed later. This is a classic Hanafi position: the condition must be fully realized while the wife is in the husband's possession for the divorce to take effect.

Key Terms
الشرط (al-shart): The condition upon which the divorce is suspended
الملك (al-milk): Ownership or possession of the wife (i.e., being in the marriage contract)
الحنث (al-hanth): Breaking of the oath (i.e., the divorce occurring)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
For a single condition in conditional divorce, its completion within the husband's ownership is sufficient to trigger the divorce, even if part occurred before marriage.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Multiple Conditions in Divorce Oaths
Intra-Hanafi dispute on multiple conditions in divorce oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf on whether multiple conditions in a divorce oath require all or any one to trigger divorce, and identify the practical consequence of their disagreement.

Core Idea
When a husband attaches multiple conditions to a divorce oath, Imam Muhammad requires all to occur; Abu Yusuf requires only one.

This passage from al-Bazzaziyya records an important intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf on the interpretation of conditional divorce oaths. The key question is whether the oath is breached by the first violation or only when all conditions are satisfied. Understanding this difference is crucial for a mufti when issuing fatwas on suspended divorces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The passage deals with a husband who says 'You are divorced if you do A and B' or 'if you do A or B'. The dispute is whether the divorce occurs upon the first condition met or only when all are met.
2
State Muhammad's position
Imam Muhammad holds that the divorce does not occur unless all conditions are fulfilled. He treats multiple conditions conjunctively, even if the particle 'or' is used, as long as the apodosis (jaza') is delayed.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
Imam Abu Yusuf holds that the divorce occurs upon the first condition met, and the oath is lifted. He sees the conditions as alternatives, so any one breach triggers the divorce.
4
Analyze the linguistic factor
The passage notes a nuance: if the apodosis (jaza') is presented (muqaddam) and the husband says 'If you eat or drink', then whichever occurs triggers divorce even according to Muhammad? Actually the passage says if he presents the apodosis, then whichever is found triggers divorce. But if he delays it, then Muhammad's view requires all. So the order matters.
The passage states: 'If he presented the apodosis, then whichever is found triggers divorce; if he delayed, Muhammad requires all.' So there is a nuance.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if the wife performs only one of the conditions, according to Muhammad she is not divorced, while according to Abu Yusuf she is divorced. This affects subsequent marital status.
Typically, Muhammad's view is preferred in later Hanafi fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي البزازية : أنت طالق إن فعلت كذا وكذا لا تطلق ما لم يوجد الكل وإن كرر حرف الشرط. إن أكلت أو شربت إن قدم الجزاء فأي شيء وجد منها يقع الطلاق وترتفع اليمين وإن أخر الطلاق لا يقع ما لم توجد الأمور على قول محمد وعلى قول أبي يوسف إذا وجد واحد يقع الطلاق ويرتفع اليمين اهـ.
And in al-Bazzaziyya: 'You are divorced if you do such and such' - she is not divorced unless all are found, even if he repeated the conditional particle. If he said 'If you eat or drink' and he presents the apodosis, then whichever is found from them, the divorce occurs and the oath is lifted; and if he delays the divorce, it does not occur unless all matters occur according to Muhammad; and according to Abu Yusuf, if one is found, the divorce occurs and the oath is lifted.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute recorded in al-Bazzaziyya regarding multiple conditions in a divorce oath. The issue: if a husband says 'You are divorced if you do A and B' (with a single conditional particle), what happens if she does only one? According to Imam Muhammad, the divorce does not occur until all conditions are fulfilled. He treats the multiple conditions as conjunctive. According to Imam Abu Yusuf, the divorce occurs upon the first condition met, and the oath is thereby exhausted. The practical difference: if she does A but not B, then according to Muhammad she is not divorced and may still violate the oath later, while according to Abu Yusuf she is divorced immediately. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not stated here, but typically in Hanafi fiqh, the view of Muhammad is preferred in such matters of oath interpretation.

Key Terms
الجزاء (al-jaza'): The apodosis or result clause of a conditional sentence
حرف الشرط (harf al-shart): The conditional particle (e.g., 'in' meaning 'if')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) She is divorced
B) She is not divorced
C) The oath is lifted
D) She is divorced only if she intended both

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, multiple conditions in a divorce oath require careful analysis: Imam Muhammad requires all conditions to be fulfilled, while Abu Yusuf triggers divorce upon the first violation, and the word order of the conditional sentence can affect the ruling.

Contradictory Oaths: Divorce, Freeing Slaves, and Oaths by Allah
Expansion on contradictory oaths from al-Muhit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the legal consequences of contradictory oaths involving divorce, freeing slaves, and oaths by Allah, and identify the key factors that determine whether an oath is broken.

Core Idea
Contradictory oaths: the legal effect depends on whether the oath has judicial bearing (talaq/itaq) or not (by Allah).

This passage from al-Muhit discusses scenarios where multiple oaths conflict—such as when a defendant swears by divorce about a debt, or when two claimants each swear ownership over a house. The rulings distinguish between oaths that have judicial effect (divorce, freeing) and those that do not (by Allah), and consider whether the oath is truthful or false. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for determining marital status and slave freedom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core distinction
Recognize that oaths by Allah have no judicial effect, whereas oaths by divorce or freeing do have judicial bearing.
This distinction is the key to understanding the rulings.
2
Analyze the debt case
Examine the scenario where the defendant swears by divorce that no debt exists, and the plaintiff provides evidence. Note the disagreement between Abu Yusuf (breaks) and Muhammad (two riwayas).
The outcome affects the wife's marital status.
3
Analyze the house ownership case
Study the case where two claimants both swear ownership, with evidence and possession. The ruling differs based on who has possession.
Both break their oaths if both have evidence and possession.
4
Analyze the sequential oaths case
Understand when a person first swears by Allah not to enter, then swears by freeing that if he did enter the slave is free. Determine why the second oath is not triggered even if entry occurred.
The first oath by Allah has no judicial effect.
5
Summarize the debt denial with payment claim
Distinguish between the defendant admitting the debt but claiming payment (no divorce) vs. denying the debt entirely (divorce occurs).
The intent behind the oath matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يناسب قوله فإن وجد الشرط طلقت ما في المحيط من باب الأيمان التي يكذب بعضها بعضاً : إذا حلف المدعى عليه بالطلاق فقال امرأته طالق إن كان لك علي ألف وبرهن المدعي وقضى به حنث الحالف عند أبي يوسف وهي رواية عن محمد، وعنه أنه لا يحنث. ولو برهن على إقرار المدعي بألف ذكر في واقعات الناطفي أنه لا يحنث. ولو حلف رجلان في أيديهما دار حلف كل أن الدار داره وبرهنا كانت بينهما ويحنثان وإن كانت في يد أحدهما حنث صاحب اليد لتقديم بينة الخارج عليه حلف بالله أنه لم يدخل هذه الدار اليوم ثم قال عبده حر إن لم يكن دخلها اليوم لا كفارة ولا يعتق عبده لأنه إن كان صادقاً في اليمين بالله تعالى لم يحنث ولا كفارة وإن كان كاذباً فهو يمين الغموس فلا توجب الكفارة واليمين بالله تعالى لا مدخل لها في القضاء فلم يصر فيها مكذباً شرعاً فلم يتحقق شرط الحنث في اليمين بالعتق وهو عدم الدخول حتى لو كانت اليمين الأولى بعتق أو طلاق حنث في اليمينين لأن لها مدخلاً في القضاء. ولو ادعى على رجل ديناً فحلف المدعى عليه بالطلاق ما له عليه شيء فأقام المدعي البينة وقضى به له ينظر ؛ إن قال كان له علي دين وأوفيته لم تطلق امرأته، وإن قال لم يكن له على شيء قط طلقت امرأته وتمامه فيه
And from what is appropriate to his statement 'if the condition is found, she is divorced' is what is in al-Muhit from the chapter of oaths that contradict each other: If the defendant swears by divorce saying 'My wife is divorced if you have a thousand against me,' and the plaintiff provides evidence and judgment is given, he (the defendant) breaks his oath according to Abu Yusuf, and it is a riwaya from Muhammad, and from him (Muhammad) that he does not break it. And if he proves the plaintiff's acknowledgment of a thousand, it is mentioned in Waqi'at al-Natafi that he does not break it. And if two men have a house in both their hands, each swears that the house is his, and they both bring evidence, it is between them and both break their oaths. If it is in the hand of one of them, the possessor breaks his oath because the external evidence is preferred over him. If he swears by Allah that he did not enter this house today, then says 'my slave is free if I did not enter it today,' there is no expiation and his slave is not freed because if he was truthful in the oath by Allah, he does not break it and no expiation; if he was lying, it is an oath of falsehood (yamin ghamus) which does not require expiation. And the oath by Allah has no bearing in judicial proceedings, so he is not considered a liar legally, and thus the condition for breaking the oath of freeing (which is non-entry) is not realized. Even if the first oath was by freeing or divorce, he breaks both oaths because they have bearing in judicial proceedings. And if someone claims a debt against a man, and the defendant swears by divorce that he owes nothing, then the plaintiff provides evidence and judgment is given for him, then it is considered: if he says 'He had a debt against me and I paid it,' his wife is not divorced; if he says 'He never had anything against me,' his wife is divorced. And the rest is in it.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This section from al-Muhit deals with cases where oaths contradict each other. The first case: a defendant swears by divorce that he does not owe money. If the plaintiff proves the debt, Abu Yusuf and one riwaya from Muhammad say the defendant breaks his oath (divorce occurs). Another riwaya from Muhammad says no. If the defendant proves an acknowledgment, no divorce. The second case: two claimants each swear they own a house. If both have evidence and both are in possession, the house is shared and both break oaths. If only one is in possession, the possessor breaks his oath because the external evidence outweighs his possession. The third case: a man swears by Allah not to enter a house, then says 'my slave is free if I entered.' Here the second oath is contingent on the first being false. The ruling: no effect because the first oath by Allah does not interact with judicial proceedings; if false, it is a sin but not legally actionable, so the condition for the freeing oath is not met. But if the first oath was also by freeing or divorce, then both break because those have judicial effect. Fourth case: a debtor swears by divorce that he owes nothing. If the plaintiff proves the debt and the debtor acknowledges it was a debt but claims payment, his wife is not divorced (he is not lying about the existence of the debt, he admits it was there). But if he denies the debt entirely, he is lying and the divorce occurs.

Key Terms
يمين الغموس (yamin al-ghamus): A false oath deliberately sworn; it is a major sin but does not require kaffarah
البينة (al-bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a man swears by Allah he did not enter a house, and then says 'my slave is free if I entered today,' and he actually entered, what happens?
A) His slave is freed
B) He must pay kaffarah
C) Neither his slave is freed nor does he owe kaffarah
D) He is divorced

Key Takeaway
Oaths by Allah in a judicial context do not trigger the consequences of conditional oaths of divorce or freeing, because they lack judicial bearing.

Conditional Divorce on Longer Life of Two Wives
Ruling on conditional divorce with two wives and impossible condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a conditional divorce hinging on which wife outlives the other and explain why it is not retroactive.

Core Idea
A conditional divorce takes effect when the condition is fulfilled, but not retroactively.

This passage from al-Qunyah addresses a nuanced scenario where a husband links divorce to the death of one of his wives. It clarifies the timing of divorce—immediate upon fulfillment of the condition—and corrects a potential misunderstanding about retroactive effect. It also touches on the effect of impossible conditions in divorce, illustrating the principle that conditions must be possible and sensible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and the ruling
The husband says to his two wives: 'The one of you who lives longer is divorced.' As long as both are alive, no divorce occurs. When one dies, the other becomes the longer-lived and is divorced immediately at that moment.
The condition is a future uncertain event (death of one wife).
2
Analyze the timing: immediate vs. retroactive
The passage explicitly states that the divorce is not retroactive (la yastanid). This means the divorce does not relate back to the time of the oath; it only takes effect at the moment of the first wife's death.
This is important because if it were retroactive, the divorce would have occurred while both were alive, which contradicts the condition.
3
Compare with Zufar's view on simultaneous death
Zufar, a Hanafi scholar, holds that if both wives die simultaneously, neither is divorced. The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and his students) does not explicitly address this scenario in the text, but it is implied that the condition fails and no divorce occurs.
This shows that intra-madhhab differences can arise in edge cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيها لو قال لامرأتين له أطولكما حياة طالق لا تطلق في الحال، فلو كانت إحداهما بنت ستين سنة والأخرى بنت عشرين سنة فماتت العجوز قبل الشابة طلقت الشابة في الحال ولا يستند خلافاً لزفر قال رحمه الله : ولو ماتتا معاً لا تطلق واحدة منهما. وإن لم تخرج الفساق من النار فأنت طالق ثلاثاً لا تطلق لتعارض الأدلة اهـ
And in it (al-Qunyah): If he said to his two wives, 'The one of you who lives longer is divorced,' she is not divorced immediately. If one is sixty years old and the other twenty, and the old one dies before the young one, the young one is divorced immediately and it is not retroactive, contrary to Zufar (may Allah have mercy on him) who said: if both die together, neither is divorced. And if he said 'If sinners are not removed from the Fire, you are divorced three times,' she is not divorced due to conflicting evidences.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage gives a case of conditional divorce involving two wives. The condition is 'the one who lives longer is divorced.' As long as both are alive, no divorce occurs. Once one dies, the other becomes the longer-lived and is divorced immediately at the death of the first, according to the Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa and his student). Zufar, a Hanafi scholar known for his independent reasoning, disagreed: he held that if both die simultaneously, neither is divorced. The case also includes an example of an impossible condition (removing sinners from Hellfire) which does not result in divorce because evidence contradicts the possibility. This illustrates that conditions that are manifestly impossible or contradictory to Islamic teachings render the divorce ineffective.

Key Terms
أطول حياة (atwal hayah): The longer-living; the condition refers to whichever wife dies later
يستند (yastanid): Retroactive; used here to say the divorce does not relate back to the time of the oath

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his two wives: 'The one who lives longer is divorced.' The older wife dies first. According to the Hanafi position, what happens?
A) The older wife is divorced
B) The younger wife is divorced immediately
C) Both are divorced
D) Neither is divorced until the second dies

Key Takeaway
A conditional divorce takes effect immediately when the condition is fulfilled, but not retroactively; impossible conditions void the divorce.

Divorce Oath Not Broken Due to Mutual Forgetfulness
Ruling on non-occurrence of condition due to mutual forgetfulness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a conditional divorce when both parties forget the condition and explain why the oath is not broken.

Core Idea
Mutual forgetfulness of an oath's condition prevents the divorce from triggering, even if the condition is literally unmet.

This short passage from al-Qunyah presents an important exception in Hanafi divorce law: when the condition of an oath is forgotten by both husband and wife, the oath is not considered broken. Understanding this ruling requires distinguishing between deliberate non-compliance and forgetfulness. We will explore the reasoning behind this exception and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Condition
The husband makes a conditional divorce: if the wife does not perform a specific act by a specific time, she is divorced. The condition is clear and time-bound.
The act is intercourse, but the ruling applies generally to any conditional act.
2
Determine What Happened
Both husband and wife forgot about the condition until after the deadline passed. The wife did not perform the act, but the omission was not deliberate.
Forgetfulness here is mutual; neither party remembered the oath.
3
Apply the Ruling
According to the passage, the husband does not break his oath. The divorce does not occur (la yahanth).
This is a specific exception to the general rule that a broken oath triggers the consequence.
4
Explain the Rationale
The Hanafi school views that the oath is broken only when the condition is violated deliberately. Forgetfulness removes the intent to violate.
This is based on the principle that actions are judged by intentions, and forgetfulness negates intentionality.
5
Note the Practical Consequence
The marriage remains intact; no divorce occurs. Additionally, the husband is not required to make any compensation (kaffarah) for a broken oath, as the oath is not considered broken.
This ruling applies only when both parties forget; if only one forgets, the ruling may differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيها : ادعا امرأته إلى الوقاع فأبت فقال متى يكون قالت غداً فقال إن لم تفعلي لي هذا المراد غداً فأنت طالق ثم نسياه حتى مضى الغد لا يحنث .اهـ.
And in it (al-Qunyah): He called his wife to intercourse, and she refused. He said, 'When will it be?' She said, 'Tomorrow.' He said, 'If you do not do this intended thing tomorrow, you are divorced.' Then both forgot until the next day passed; he does not break the oath.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This is another example from al-Qunyah where the oath is not broken despite the literal condition being unmet. The husband demanded a specific act by a specific time; but both parties forgot. The ruling is that the divorce does not occur because the non-fulfillment was not due to the wife's deliberate avoidance but due to forgetfulness that affected both parties. This is an exception to the general rule that if the condition is not met, the divorce occurs. The rationale is likely that the oath was conditional on the wife's act, but her forgetfulness is excusable.

Key Terms
نسي (nasiya): To forget; used here for both parties forgetting the oath
الوقاع (al-wiqaa'): Sexual intercourse
حنث (hantha): To break an oath; opposite of being faithful to an oath

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife: 'If you do not do X tomorrow, you are divorced.' Both forget until after tomorrow passes. What is the ruling according to the passage?
A) Divorce occurs.
B) Divorce does not occur.
C) Husband must pay kaffarah.
D) The wife must make qada of the act.

Key Takeaway
Mutual forgetfulness of the condition in a conditional divorce prevents the divorce from taking effect, as it is not considered a deliberate violation.

Dissolution of Oath by Condition Occurring Outside Marriage
Rule: condition outside marriage dissolves oath, no divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule regarding a conditional divorce when the condition occurs outside the husband's possession and explain why the oath dissolves.

Core Idea
If the condition for divorce occurs when the wife is not in your marriage, no divorce falls and the oath dissolves.

This lesson examines a nuanced point in Hanafi law concerning suspended divorces (ta'liq al-talaq). The key distinction is whether the condition occurs while the wife is in the husband's possession (within the marriage) or outside it. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that if the condition occurs outside possession, the divorce does not take effect and the oath that suspended it is dissolved. However, if the condition never occurs at all (even within possession), the oath remains pending. Understanding this distinction is vital for correctly applying conditional divorce clauses.

Guided Path
1
Classify the legal act
Identify that this is a conditional talaq (ta'liq) – a divorce suspended on a future condition. The statement creates an oath (yamin) that the divorce will occur if the condition is met.
In Hanafi, suspended divorces are considered valid oaths.
2
Determine the timing of the condition
Ask: did the condition occur while the wife was legally in the husband's possession (i.e., during the marriage) or did it occur outside that period (e.g., before marriage or after divorce)?
Possession (milk) means the marital bond exists at the moment of occurrence.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule
If the condition occurs outside possession: no divorce takes place, and the oath is dissolved (inhilal al-yamin). The husband is freed from the oath and no talaq is recorded.
This is the explicit ruling from the text.
4
Contrast with non-occurrence
Distinguish this from the case where the condition simply never occurs at all (even within possession). In that case, the oath is not dissolved; it remains pending until death or explicit cancellation.
The text says: 'but by mere non-existence of the condition in his possession, it is not dissolved.'
5
Summarize the practical consequence
The husband is no longer bound by the oath; the wife's marital status is unaffected. No further action is needed to cancel the condition.
This provides legal certainty for the parties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلا لا وانحلت أي إن لم يوجد الشرط في الملك لا يقع الطلاق وتنحل اليمين إن وجد في غير الملك، وأما بمجرد عدم الشرط في الملك لا تنحل .
And if not, then it is void and dissolved: that is, if the condition is not found in his possession, the divorce does not occur, and the oath is dissolved if it is found outside his possession; but by mere non-existence of the condition in his possession, it is not dissolved.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This is a summary rule. The author clarifies a crucial distinction: if the condition occurs (i.e., the act happens) but does so when the wife is not in the husband's possession (e.g., before marriage or after divorce), then the divorce does not occur, and the oath is dissolved (the contingency ends). However, if the condition simply does not occur at all (even within possession), the oath is not automatically dissolved; it remains pending until death or cancellation. The key is that the oath is dissolved only if the condition is actually met, albeit outside ownership.

Key Terms
انحلال اليمين (inhilal al-yamin): Dissolution of the oath; the conditional divorce no longer hangs over the marriage
الشرط في الملك (al-shart fi al-milk): The occurrence of the condition while the wife is in the husband's possession

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a condition for divorce occurs when the wife is not in the husband's marriage (e.g., before marriage), what happens?
A) The divorce occurs
B) The divorce does not occur and the oath is dissolved
C) The divorce does not occur but the oath remains
D) The husband must renew the oath after marriage

Key Takeaway
The occurrence of a condition outside the husband's possession dissolves the oath and prevents divorce from falling.

Legal Capacity in Conditional Divorce: The Time of the Oath Matters
Ruling on the effect of insanity on validity of conditional divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the effect of insanity on a conditional divorce and identify the critical time for legal capacity.

Core Idea
For a conditional divorce to be valid, the husband must be sane at the moment he makes the oath—not when the condition occurs.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a subtle but crucial point in Hanafi family law: legal capacity (ahliyyah) is required at the time of suspending the divorce, not at the time the condition is fulfilled. Understanding this distinction prevents common errors in applying conditional divorce rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
Recognize that the ruling concerns ta'liq al-talaq (suspending divorce on a condition). The key is the moment of making the suspension, not the moment the condition is met.
2
Identify the condition for validity
The husband must be sane (possessing legal capacity) at the time he makes the oath (waqt al-ta'liq). This is the sole condition mentioned in the passage.
Insanity at the time of the condition does not affect validity.
3
Apply the two scenarios
Consider both possibilities: (1) sane at oath, later insane at condition → divorce valid and occurs; (2) insane at oath, later sane → oath invalid, no divorce even if condition met.
The passage explicitly states these outcomes.
4
Conclude the ruling
The ruling is that legal capacity is a condition for the validity of the ta'liq itself. It is not required at the time of the condition.
This is agreed upon in the Radawiyya method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أنه تعتبر الأهلية وقت التعليق قال في القنية : وفي الطريقة الرضوية أجمعنا أن الأهلية في تعليق الطلاق تعتبر وقت اليمين لا وقت الشرط حتى لو كان مفيقاً وقت اليمين مجنوناً وقت الشرط يصح ويقع وعلى العكس لا يصح اليمين اهـ .
Then know that legal capacity is considered at the time of suspension. He said in al-Qunyah: In al-Tariqa al-Radawiyya, we have agreed that capacity in suspending divorce is considered at the time of the oath, not at the time of the condition. So if he was sane at the time of the oath and insane at the time of the condition, the oath is valid and divorce occurs. And vice versa, the oath is not valid.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage establishes a key Hanafi principle: the validity of a conditional divorce depends on the husband's legal capacity (aql, sanity) at the moment he makes the oath, not when the condition is fulfilled. If he was sane when he said 'if you enter the house, you are divorced,' but later becomes insane, the divorce still occurs when she enters. Conversely, if he was insane when he made the oath, it is invalid even if he regains sanity later. This is because the suspension (ta'liq) is an act that requires intention and understanding, which only a sane person can have.

Key Terms
الأهلية (al-ahliyyah): Legal capacity; specifically sanity at the time of the oath
وقت التعليق (waqt al-ta'liq): The time when the husband makes the conditional divorce statement

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A sane husband says 'If you go to the market, you are divorced.' He then becomes insane, and the wife goes to the market. What is the ruling?
A) The divorce does not occur because he is insane at the time of the condition
B) The divorce occurs because he was sane at the time of the oath
C) The divorce is suspended pending his recovery
D) The oath is invalid because he is insane

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the validity of a conditional divorce hinges on the husband's legal capacity at the moment he makes the oath, not when the condition is fulfilled.

Husband's Word in Condition Disputes
Ruling with proof: husband's word in condition disputes; exception for mudaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mu'allaq and mudaf divorce and apply the rule of whose word is accepted in disputes about condition occurrence and timing.

Core Idea
For mu'allaq divorce, the husband's word is accepted; for mudaf divorce, the wife's word is accepted regarding timing.

In disputes between husband and wife about whether a condition for divorce occurred, the Hanafi school applies a nuanced rule. The key distinction is between conditional (mu'allaq) and time-based (mudaf) divorce. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the rule and its rationale, including an exception for mudaf divorce where the wife's word is accepted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of dispute
Determine whether the disagreement is about the occurrence of a condition (mu'allaq) or about the timing of a future divorce (mudaf).
2
Apply the default rule for mu'allaq
If the dispute is about whether a condition occurred, the husband's word is accepted because he is the denier and the original state is absence of divorce.
This holds even if apparent evidence supports the wife.
3
Recognize the exception for mudaf
If the divorce is mudaf (added to a future time) and the dispute is about timing, the wife's word is accepted because the mudaf divorce is a cause in the present with delayed effect.
Example: 'You are divorced for the sunna' – wife's claim about purity is accepted.
4
Understand the rationale
The husband's word is preferred in mu'allaq because he denies the divorce; the wife's word is preferred in mudaf because she claims the timing has arrived and the mudaf is a cause in the present.
The passage refines a common justification based on 'holding onto the original state'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن اختلفا في وجود الشرط فالقول له) أي للزوج لأنه منكر وقوع الطلاق وهي تدعيه وهذا أولى من التعليل بأنه متمسك بالأصل لأن الأصل عدم الشرط والقول لمن يتمسك بالأصل لأن الظاهر شاهد له اهـ . لأنه لا يشمل ما إذا كان الظاهر شاهداً لها والحكم قبول قوله مطلقاً فلذا لو قال لها إن لم تدخلي هذه الدار اليوم فأنت طالق فقالت لم أدخلها وقال الزوج بل دخلتيها فالقول له وإن كان الظاهر شاهداً لها وهو أن الأصل عدم الدخول لكونه منكراً. وأقوى سنة لو قال لها إن لم أجامعك في حيضتك فالقول له أنه جامعها مع الظاهر شاهد لها من وجهين كون الأصل عدم العارض وكون الحرمة مانعة له من الجماع. قيد بالشرط لأن الاختلاف لو كان في وقت المضاف كان القول لها كما إذا قال لها أنت طالق للسنة ثم قال جامعتك وهي طاهرة لا يقبل قوله بخلاف ما إذا كانت حائضاً لأنه يمكنه إنشاء الجماع فيه وإن لم يجز شرعاً، أما إذا كانت طاهرة فلكونه اعترف بالسبب لما قدمنا أن المضاف ينعقد سبباً للحال بخلاف المعلق. وفي الكافي من هذا الباب : لو قال لامرأته الموطوءة أنت طالق للسنة لا يقع إلا في طهر حال عن الطلاق والوطء عقيب حيض عن الطلاق والوطء فإذا حاضت وطهرت وادعى الزوج جماعها وطلاقها في الحيض لا يقبل قوله في منع الطلاق السني لانعقاد المضاف سبباً للحال وإنما يتراخى حكمه فقط، فدعوى الطلاق أو الجماع بعده دعوى المانع فلا يقبل قوله في منع وقوع الطلاق في الطهر لكن يقع طلاق آخر بإقراره بالطلاق في الحيض وإن ادعى الطلاق أو الجماع وهي حائض قوله : (طلقت الشابة في الحال حاصله أنه ما دامتا حيتين لا يقع شيء، وإن ماتت واحدة منهما تكون الباقية أطولهما حياة، ولا ينظر إلى السن كما في التتارخانية عن اليتيمة قال : وأنشد لنا شعراً : وإن حياة المرء بعد عدوه ولو ساعة من عمره لكثير
His statement: (And if they differ about the existence of the condition, the word is his) i.e., the husband's, because he is the denier of the occurrence of divorce while she claims it. This is better than the rationale that he is holding onto the original state, because the original state is the absence of the condition, and the word is given to the one holding onto the original state because the apparent evidence supports him. Thus, it does not cover the case where the apparent evidence supports her, and the ruling is that his word is accepted absolutely. Hence, if he said to her, 'If you do not enter this house today, you are divorced,' and she said 'I did not enter it,' and the husband said 'You entered it,' his word is accepted, even though the apparent evidence supports her, because the original state is non-entry, but he is the denier. And even stronger: if he said to her, 'If I do not have intercourse with you during your menses,' his word is accepted that he did have intercourse, even though the apparent evidence supports her from two aspects: the original state is non-occurrence of the event, and the prohibition prevents him from intercourse. The restriction to condition is because if the disagreement is about the time of the added (mudaf) divorce, the word is hers, as when he says to her 'You are divorced for the sunna' and then he says 'I had intercourse with you while you were pure,' his word is not accepted, unlike if she was menstruating, because he can initiate intercourse during menstruation even though it is not permissible legally. As for when she is pure, it is because he has admitted the cause, as we have established that the added (mudaf) is a cause in the present, unlike the suspended (mu'allaq). And in al-Kafi from this chapter: if he said to his wife with whom he has had intercourse, 'You are divorced for the sunna,' it only occurs in a purity free from divorce and intercourse, after menstruation free from divorce and intercourse. So when she menstruates and becomes pure, and the husband claims he had intercourse with her and divorced her during her menses, his word is not accepted to prevent the sunni divorce, because the added is a cause in the present, and its effect is merely delayed. So his claim of divorce or intercourse afterwards is a claim of a preventive factor, so his word is not accepted to prevent the occurrence of divorce in purity; but another divorce occurs by his acknowledgment of divorce during menses. And if he claims divorce or intercourse while she is menstruating, his statement (the young wife is divorced immediately) its summary is that as long as both are alive, nothing occurs; if one dies, the remaining is the longer-living, and age is not considered, as in al-Tatar Khaniyya from al-Yatimah, who said: and he recited poetry to us: 'The life of a man after his enemy, even for an hour of his life, is much.'
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This long passage covers the principle of whose word is taken when the husband and wife disagree about whether the condition for divorce occurred. The base rule: the husband's word is taken because he denies the divorce. The author refines this: even if the apparent evidence (zahir) supports the wife, the husband's word is taken because he is the denier. Example: he says, 'If you do not enter the house,' she says she didn't enter, he says she did; his word is accepted. Then a stronger case: he swears that he did not have intercourse with her during her menses; his word that he did have intercourse is accepted despite two apparent proofs against him (original absence of intercourse and prohibition). Then an exception: if the divorce is mudaf (added to a future time) rather than mu'allaq (suspended on a condition), the word is given to the wife if the dispute is about timing. Example: he says 'You are divorced for the sunna' (i.e., at a future purity period). If he later claims he had intercourse with her during her purity, his word is not accepted because the mudaf divorce is a cause in the present and delays only the effect. The passage also includes a reference to al-Kafi about the conditions for a sunni divorce. Finally, it returns to the two wives case, summarizing the ruling: as long as both are alive, no divorce; if one dies, the other is divorced immediately; no consideration of age (i.e., the younger is not necessarily the longer-living).

Key Terms
المضاف (al-mudaf): A divorce assigned to a future time (e.g., 'you are divorced tomorrow')
المعلق (al-mu'allaq): A divorce suspended on a condition (e.g., 'if you enter the house, you are divorced')
السنة (al-sunnah): The proper (sunni) divorce: a single revocable divorce pronounced during the wife's purity period without intercourse

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife: 'You are divorced for the sunna (i.e., at the next purity period).' She claims she is pure and he claims he had intercourse with her during her purity. Who is believed?
A) The husband
B) The wife
C) Both are believed equally
D) Neither; a judge decides

Key Takeaway
In disputes about condition occurrence for mu'allaq divorce, the husband's word is accepted; for mudaf divorce, the wife's word is accepted regarding timing.

Two Wives with Conditional Talaq on Death
Ruling on conditional talaq involving two wives and death as condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on conditional divorce involving two wives when the condition is tied to death and apply it to determine when divorce occurs.

Core Idea
If a husband says 'the younger of you is divorced immediately', and both are alive, no divorce occurs; if one dies, the other is divorced immediately, regardless of age.

This passage presents a specific case of conditional talaq involving two wives. The condition is death of one wife, triggering divorce for the other. The ruling emphasizes that no divorce occurs as long as both are alive, and upon death, the surviving wife is divorced immediately without regard to age. This clarifies the operation of mu'allaq talaq in a multi-wife scenario.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional statement
The husband says: 'The younger of you is divorced immediately' (or similar phrasing linking divorce to death of the other).
The condition is death of the other wife.
2
Determine the status while both are alive
As long as both wives are alive, the condition has not occurred, so no divorce takes effect.
3
Apply the ruling upon death of one wife
When one wife dies, the remaining wife is divorced immediately, regardless of which one was younger or older.
Age is not considered; the surviving wife is divorced.
4
Understand the justification
The ruling is based on the general principle of mu'allaq talaq: divorce is suspended until the condition is fulfilled. Here the condition is death of a specific wife, but the wording 'younger' does not control the outcome; it is simply part of the condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن اختلفا في وجود الشرط فالقول له) أي للزوج لأنه منكر وقوع الطلاق وهي تدعيه وهذا أولى من التعليل بأنه متمسك بالأصل لأن الأصل عدم الشرط والقول لمن يتمسك بالأصل لأن الظاهر شاهد له اهـ . لأنه لا يشمل ما إذا كان الظاهر شاهداً لها والحكم قبول قوله مطلقاً فلذا لو قال لها إن لم تدخلي هذه الدار اليوم فأنت طالق فقالت لم أدخلها وقال الزوج بل دخلتيها فالقول له وإن كان الظاهر شاهداً لها وهو أن الأصل عدم الدخول لكونه منكراً. وأقوى سنة لو قال لها إن لم أجامعك في حيضتك فالقول له أنه جامعها مع الظاهر شاهد لها من وجهين كون الأصل عدم العارض وكون الحرمة مانعة له من الجماع. قيد بالشرط لأن الاختلاف لو كان في وقت المضاف كان القول لها كما إذا قال لها أنت طالق للسنة ثم قال جامعتك وهي طاهرة لا يقبل قوله بخلاف ما إذا كانت حائضاً لأنه يمكنه إنشاء الجماع فيه وإن لم يجز شرعاً، أما إذا كانت طاهرة فلكونه اعترف بالسبب لما قدمنا أن المضاف ينعقد سبباً للحال بخلاف المعلق. وفي الكافي من هذا الباب : لو قال لامرأته الموطوءة أنت طالق للسنة لا يقع إلا في طهر حال عن الطلاق والوطء عقيب حيض عن الطلاق والوطء فإذا حاضت وطهرت وادعى الزوج جماعها وطلاقها في الحيض لا يقبل قوله في منع الطلاق السني لانعقاد المضاف سبباً للحال وإنما يتراخى حكمه فقط، فدعوى الطلاق أو الجماع بعده دعوى المانع فلا يقبل قوله في منع وقوع الطلاق في الطهر لكن يقع طلاق آخر بإقراره بالطلاق في الحيض وإن ادعى الطلاق أو الجماع وهي حائض قوله : (طلقت الشابة في الحال حاصله أنه ما دامتا حيتين لا يقع شيء، وإن ماتت واحدة منهما تكون الباقية أطولهما حياة، ولا ينظر إلى السن كما في التتارخانية عن اليتيمة قال : وأنشد لنا شعراً : وإن حياة المرء بعد عدوه ولو ساعة من عمره لكثير
His statement: (And if they differ about the existence of the condition, the word is his) i.e., the husband's, because he is the denier of the occurrence of divorce while she claims it. This is better than the rationale that he is holding onto the original state, because the original state is the absence of the condition, and the word is given to the one holding onto the original state because the apparent evidence supports him. Thus, it does not cover the case where the apparent evidence supports her, and the ruling is that his word is accepted absolutely. Hence, if he said to her, 'If you do not enter this house today, you are divorced,' and she said 'I did not enter it,' and the husband said 'You entered it,' his word is accepted, even though the apparent evidence supports her, because the original state is non-entry, but he is the denier. And even stronger: if he said to her, 'If I do not have intercourse with you during your menses,' his word is accepted that he did have intercourse, even though the apparent evidence supports her from two aspects: the original state is non-occurrence of the event, and the prohibition prevents him from intercourse. The restriction to condition is because if the disagreement is about the time of the added (mudaf) divorce, the word is hers, as when he says to her 'You are divorced for the sunna' and then he says 'I had intercourse with you while you were pure,' his word is not accepted, unlike if she was menstruating, because he can initiate intercourse during menstruation even though it is not permissible legally. As for when she is pure, it is because he has admitted the cause, as we have established that the added (mudaf) is a cause in the present, unlike the suspended (mu'allaq). And in al-Kafi from this chapter: if he said to his wife with whom he has had intercourse, 'You are divorced for the sunna,' it only occurs in a purity free from divorce and intercourse, after menstruation free from divorce and intercourse. So when she menstruates and becomes pure, and the husband claims he had intercourse with her and divorced her during her menses, his word is not accepted to prevent the sunni divorce, because the added is a cause in the present, and its effect is merely delayed. So his claim of divorce or intercourse afterwards is a claim of a preventive factor, so his word is not accepted to prevent the occurrence of divorce in purity; but another divorce occurs by his acknowledgment of divorce during menses. And if he claims divorce or intercourse while she is menstruating, his statement (the young wife is divorced immediately) its summary is that as long as both are alive, nothing occurs; if one dies, the remaining is the longer-living, and age is not considered, as in al-Tatar Khaniyya from al-Yatimah, who said: and he recited poetry to us: 'The life of a man after his enemy, even for an hour of his life, is much.'
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This long passage covers the principle of whose word is taken when the husband and wife disagree about whether the condition for divorce occurred. The base rule: the husband's word is taken because he denies the divorce. The author refines this: even if the apparent evidence (zahir) supports the wife, the husband's word is taken because he is the denier. Example: he says, 'If you do not enter the house,' she says she didn't enter, he says she did; his word is accepted. Then a stronger case: he swears that he did not have intercourse with her during her menses; his word that he did have intercourse is accepted despite two apparent proofs against him (original absence of intercourse and prohibition). Then an exception: if the divorce is mudaf (added to a future time) rather than mu'allaq (suspended on a condition), the word is given to the wife if the dispute is about timing. Example: he says 'You are divorced for the sunna' (i.e., at a future purity period). If he later claims he had intercourse with her during her purity, his word is not accepted because the mudaf divorce is a cause in the present and delays only the effect. The passage also includes a reference to al-Kafi about the conditions for a sunni divorce. Finally, it returns to the two wives case, summarizing the ruling: as long as both are alive, no divorce; if one dies, the other is divorced immediately; no consideration of age (i.e., the younger is not necessarily the longer-living).

Key Terms
المعلق (al-mu'allaq): A divorce suspended on a condition (e.g., 'if you enter the house, you are divorced')
الشابة (al-shābba): The young wife, but in this context part of the condition's phrasing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his two wives: 'The younger of you is divorced when the older dies.' Both are alive. Then the older dies. Who is divorced?
A) The younger wife
B) The older wife
C) No one
D) Both are divorced

Key Takeaway
In conditional talaq linked to death of a wife, no divorce occurs while both are alive; upon death, the surviving wife is divorced immediately, regardless of age.

Conditional Divorce with Impossible or Contradictory Conditions
Rulings on conditional divorce when condition is impossible or contradictory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a conditional divorce involves an impossible or contradictory condition, and identify the principle that prevents divorce.

Core Idea
If the condition is impossible or contradictory, the divorce does not occur.

This lesson examines two specific cases from the Hanafi school where a conditional divorce is ineffective. The first involves a man who conditionally divorces two wives and they both die simultaneously. The second involves a condition that is impossible by nature or revelation. In both, the divorce is not effected, due to the impossibility of fulfilling the condition or the conflict of evidences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage gives two distinct cases: simultaneous death of two wives in a suspended divorce, and an impossible condition (removing sinners from Hell).
2
Understand the legal reasoning for the first
Divorce requires living spouses; death makes the condition impossible, so divorce does not occur.
The condition cannot be applied after death.
3
Understand the legal reasoning for the second
The condition is impossible, and the evidences (the conditional wording vs. impossibility) conflict, so the original state (no divorce) is upheld.
Hanafi usul: when evidences conflict, istishab prevails.
4
Apply the overarching principle
The Hanafi rule that when a condition cannot be met or when dalil are contradictory, the divorce is not valid.
This principle applies to any impossible condition, not just the examples given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ولو ماتتا معاً لا تطلق واحدة منهما. وإن لم تخرج الفساق من النار فأنت طالق ثلاثاً لا تطلق لتعارض الأدلة
He (the author) said: And if both die together, neither of them is divorced. And if she does not remove the corrupt from the Fire, you are thrice divorced; she is not divorced due to conflicting evidences.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings. First: If a man conditionally divorces two wives (e.g., 'If I divorce you, you are divorced') and both die simultaneously, the divorce does not occur because the condition (divorce) cannot be applied after death. Second: If the condition is impossible by reason or revelation (like removing sinners from Hell), the divorce is not effective because the evidences conflict, and the original state (no divorce) remains.

Key Terms
تعارض الأدلة (ta‘āruḍ al-adilla): Contradiction of evidences such that neither prevails, so the original ruling (no divorce) remains.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says to his two wives 'If I divorce you, you are divorced' and both die at the same time, what is the ruling?
A) Both are divorced
B) Neither is divorced
C) The younger one is divorced
D) It is suspended until resurrection

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, conditional divorce fails if the condition is impossible or if evidences conflict, leaving the marriage intact.

Dispute Over Condition for Divorce: Husband's Word Accepted
Commentary explaining the reasoning for accepting the husband's word.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on whose word is accepted when spouses dispute the occurrence of a condition and explain the preferred legal reasoning behind it.

Core Idea
The husband's word is accepted because he is the denier of divorce.

In Hanafi family law, when a husband conditions divorce on an event and the spouses later differ on whether the event occurred, a key procedural question arises: whose statement is given weight? This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that the husband's denial is accepted, and explains the preferred reasoning. We will unpack this ruling and its foundation in the principles of judicial procedure (qada).

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
The husband made divorce conditional on an event. They differ: the wife claims the condition occurred (she was divorced), the husband denies it.
The core is who is the claimant (mudda'i) and who is the denier (munkir).
2
Determine claimant and denier
The wife claims the divorce happened, so she is the mudda'i. The husband denies, so he is the munkir.
In Hanafi procedure, the claimant must prove, the denier takes an oath.
3
State the rule
The word (al-qawl) is given to the husband because he is the denier.
This is the mufta bihi position.
4
Recognize the two proofs offered
First proof: husband denies the occurrence. Second proof: husband holds onto the original state (asl) of no condition.
The commentator prefers the first over the second.
5
Understand the preferred reasoning
The denier's proof is more direct and avoids potential pitfalls of the origin argument (e.g., when the origin is ambiguous).
This aligns with the maxim: 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' but here it's about burden of proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن اختلفا في وجود الشرط فالقول له أي للزوج لأنه منكر وقوع الطلاق وهي تدعيه وهذا أولى من التعليل بأنه متمسك بالأصل لأن الأصل عدم الشرط والقول لمن يتمسك بالأصل لأن الظاهر شاهد له
His saying: 'And if they differ regarding the existence of the condition, the word is his, i.e., the husband's, because he denies the occurrence of divorce while she claims it. And this is preferable to the reasoning that he is holding onto the origin, because the origin is the absence of the condition, and the word is for the one who holds onto the origin because the apparent evidence is for him.'
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi rule on disputes about whether the condition for divorce actually occurred. The husband's denial is accepted because he is the one denying the occurrence of divorce. The commentator prefers this reasoning over saying it's because he upholds the default (original) state, though that also supports him. The key is that the husband is the defendant (munkar) and the wife is the claimant (mudda‘iya), so the burden of proof is on her.

Key Terms
المنكر (al-munkir): The one who denies the occurrence, here the husband.
المدعي (al-mudda'i): The one who claims the occurrence, here the wife.
الأصل (al-asl): The original state of affairs (here, no condition).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When the husband and wife dispute whether the condition for divorce occurred, whose word is accepted?
A) The wife's, because she is the one affected
B) The husband's, because he denies the divorce
C) Neither, they must bring witnesses
D) The judge decides based on custom

Key Takeaway
In disputes over a divorce condition, the husband's denial is accepted with oath because he is the denier, shifting the burden of proof to the wife.

Denial of Condition in Conditional Divorce: Husband's Claim Accepted
Ruling on denial of condition in suspended divorce with qiyas reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that a husband's denial of the condition in a suspended divorce is accepted, and explain the qiyas reasoning behind it.

Core Idea
Denying the condition is denying the cause – the husband's claim of fulfilment is accepted.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expounds a key principle in conditional divorce. When a husband suspends divorce on an explicit condition (sarih al-shart), the divorce occurs only upon the condition's fulfilment. If the husband later claims he fulfilled the condition but the wife disputes, the husband's denial of the condition is accepted because he is effectively denying the cause (sabab) of divorce. This ruling is based on a qiyas: just as one who denies the cause of an obligation is believed, so here the husband's statement is taken as true, preserving the original state (absence of divorce).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the condition
Recognize that the husband has suspended divorce on an explicit condition (sarih al-shart) such as 'if I do not have intercourse during your menstruation'.
Explicit condition means clear conditional language.
2
Understand the principle of ta'liq
The divorce only occurs when the condition is fulfilled. The condition is the cause (sabab) of divorce.
No divorce without fulfilment of condition.
3
Identify the nature of the dispute
The husband claims he fulfilled the condition (e.g., had intercourse); the wife denies. The husband is denying the existence of the unfulfilled condition.
The husband says 'the condition did not remain unfulfilled', i.e., he denies the cause.
4
Apply the legal reasoning
Since the condition is the cause, denying the condition is denying the cause. Denying the cause negates the effect (divorce). The husband's statement is accepted because the original state is no divorce and his denial of the cause preserves that state.
Qiyas: denial of cause negates effect, similar to other obligations.
5
State the ruling
The husband's statement is accepted, and the divorce does not occur. This applies to any explicit condition in suspended divorce.
The ruling is consistent across the two examples given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صدق. ولو قال إن لم أجامعك في حيضتك فأنت طالق فادعى الجماع في الحيض لا تطلق لأنه علق الطلاق بصريح الشرط والمعلق بالشرط إنما ينعقد سبياً. عند الشرط لما عرف، فإذا أنكر الشرط فقد أنكر السبب فيقبل قوله . وكذا لو قال إن لم أقربك في أربعة أشهر فأنت طالق فمضت المدة ثم ادعى القربان في المدة لا يقع لأنه علق الطلاق بصريح الشرط فمتى أنكر الشرط فقد أنكر السبب فيقبل قوله.
He said: And if he said, 'If I do not have intercourse with you during your menstruation, then you are divorced,' and he claims intercourse during the menstruation, she is not divorced because he suspended divorce with an explicit condition, and what is suspended on a condition only occurs at the occurrence of the condition, as is known. So if he denies the condition, he has denied the cause, so his statement is accepted. Likewise, if he said, 'If I do not approach you for four months, then you are divorced,' and the period passes, then he claims approach during the period, it does not occur because he suspended divorce with an explicit condition, so whenever he denies the condition, he denied the cause, so his statement is accepted.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on disputes in conditional divorce. The passage is a ruling with proof — specifically, it explains why the husband's denial of the condition is believed. The key principle: When divorce is suspended on a condition, the occurrence of the divorce depends on the existence of the condition. If the husband denies the condition, he is effectively denying the cause (sabab) of divorce, and his statement is accepted because the original state is the absence of divorce. The proof is qiyas: just as one who denies the cause of an obligation is believed, so here. The ruling applies to explicit conditions (sarih al-shart). Two examples are given: (1) conditional on not having intercourse during menstruation, and (2) conditional on not approaching for four months. In both, if the husband claims he fulfilled the condition, his statement is accepted, and divorce does not occur.

Key Terms
التعليق بالشرط (al-ta'liq bi al-shart): Suspending the occurrence of divorce on the occurrence of a future condition.
صريح الشرط (sarih al-shart): An explicitly stated condition using clear words of condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says, 'If I do not have intercourse with you during your menstruation, you are divorced,' and the wife claims the condition occurred but the husband denies it, what is the ruling?
A) The wife's claim is accepted because she is the plaintiff.
B) The husband's denial is accepted because he denies the cause of divorce.
C) Both are equally credible and the case goes to qasamah.
D) The divorce occurs immediately because the condition is presumed fulfilled.

Key Takeaway
In conditional divorce, the husband's denial of the condition is accepted because it denies the cause of divorce, preserving the original state of no divorce.
